745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 4 
 
 
1.  Excellence
1.1  Objectives  
With the rapid growth and development of the human population, municipal solid waste (MSW) has contrib uted significantly to 
the total amount of waste generated by our society. Today in Europe, each person generates, in average, 475 kg of MSW every 
year 1, this amount increasing at an annual rate of 10%. Around 40-50% of this amount correspond to bio-wast e (food and 
kitchen waste from households, restaurants, caterer s and retail premises, comparable waste from market  areas and 
biodegradable garden and park waste), 2 so that a total biomass of more than 100 million t ons derived from MSW is yearly 
produced in the EU. There are two major streams: gr een waste/pruning residues (including 50-60% water and lignocellulosic 
materials) and kitchen waste (no wood and up to 80%  water). This organic fraction mainly contains carb ohydrates (30-40% 
cellulose, hemicellulose, starch, pectins), protein s (5-15%) and lipids (10-15%), which are all useful  raw material that can be 
converted to biobased products. The valorisation of  organic MSW not only helps to solve environmental pollution but also 
contributes to the transition from a linear to a renewable circular economy. 
Due to the increasingly restrictive MSW management legislation 3 in the last years there has been a strong shift fr om MSW 
landfilling to recycling of materials such as glass , plastics, paper or metals (11 %) and energy produ ction from the combustible 
fraction through incineration (26%). Digestion and composting (16%) have also contributed to the reduc tion of the 
biodegradable fraction of MSW sent to landfill 1. It is estimated that the AD of the total organic fraction of municipal solid waste 
(OFMSW) produced in the world could generate enough  biogas to contribute around 2% of the global energ y consumption. 
However, despite this great AD potential to recover  energy, conventional AD only generates single low- value products, biogas 
and compost (Figure 1.1a). Almost 20 years later th e Landfill Directive 1999/31/EC, and after the publ ication of the revised 
Waste Framework Directive 2008/98/EC it is mandatory to improve the management of bio-waste in EU by supporting technical 
solutions that are able to generate higher value products.

Figure 1.1a Current scenario for MSW treatment.  
 
This trend has been recently reinforced through the recent publication of the Circular Economy package COM(2015) 614 final of 
December 2, 2015 , Closing the loop an EU Action Plan for the Circular  Economy (http://ec.europa.eu/environment/circular-
economy/index_en.htm) that includes the proposal fo r the modification of the Landfill, Waste Framework  and Packaging 
Directives among others. A careful analysis of the baseline situation at EU-28 level about generation,  collection, management 
and related policies about the MSW will be the star ting point to evaluate how this biowaste flow could  be diverted from actual 
local management systems into the proposed biorefinery/valorisation chain.  
The aim of URBIOFIN project is to demonstrate the t echno-economic and environmental viability of the conversion of 
10 tonnes per day of the organic fraction of MSW (OFMSW) into: 
 Chemical building blocks  (bioethanol, medium or short volatile fatty acids, biogas), 
 Biopolymers  (low and medium chain polyhydroxyalcanoates, composites combining different PHA) 
 Additives  (bioethylene, microalgae derived biochemicals). 
By using the biorefinery concept applied to MSW (urban biorefinery), URBIOFIN will exploit the OFMSW as feedstock, 
taking into account its heterogeneity and variable composition, to produce at semi-industrial scale di fferent valuable 
marketable products (Table 1.1a). The processes inv olved in the development of these bioproducts will be 
interconnected so that a versatile and efficient bi orefinery for OFMSW will be demonstrated (Figure 1. 1b,c). 
URBIOFIN will offer a new feasible and more sustainable scenario alternative to the current treatment of the OFMSW. 
The project will validate the whole value chain (Fi gure 1.1b) at demonstration scale, taking into acco unt the involvement of 
waste management authorities (URBASER and Municipal ities) and the validation of the targeted products by end-users (SES,

approach. At the same time the objectives of the project are aligned to RIS3 strategies from all European regions involved.

Therefore the evolution of the Figure 1.1a througho ut this project will be to the following figure 1.1 c . In contrast to the 
current MSW treatment, the URBIOFIN concept (see fi gure 1.1c) proposes a global approach that regards OFMSW as a 
promising new raw material for a higher added-value  production. Thus, the URBIOFIN concept consists of an integrated 
biorefinery for the valorisation of OFMSW to high-value products treating at least 10 tonnes per day (during the project 
will be processed more than 3,000 tonnes), such pol yhydroxyalcanoates (PHA) and bioethylene and chemical building 
blocks as bioethanol, volatile fatty acids and biog as.  In this way the URBIOFIN concept  will include the  following 
improvements to the baseline treatment plant: (1) t he two-phase AD unit, aimed to obtain volatile fatt y acids (VFAs) in the first 
stage and also biogas as chemical building block in the second stage to produce PHA or biomethane (as final fuel for vehicles); 
(2) use of the bioethanol produced from the OFMSW f or elongation of VFA to medium chain fatty acids (M CFA) that could 
represent a final product for cosmetic applications  or a precursor for PHA production 3) Use of the bi oethanol to produce 
Ethylene for fruit ripening  4) Use the VFA as carbon-source for PHA enrichment step 5) Use of the MCFA as carbon-source for 
PHA accumulation as medium chain length PHA via pure culture. 6) Use of the VFA as carbon-source for PHA accumulation as 
short chain length polyhydroxyalcanoates and scl-PH A products via mixed culture 7) Use of the digestat e from the second 
stage and the residual biomass from the later PHAs process to obtain solid fertilizers 8) Use of the microalgae biomass from the 
biogas upgrading process to produce aminoacids for high-value liquid fertilizers 9) Use of the biogas as carbon-source for PHA 
production in order to obtain bioplastics to be use d for packaging/agriculture/household bags and 10) the mentioned mcl-PHA 
and scl-PHA’s in combination with various cellulose - and  lignin-containing pulp and other solid mater ials deriving from MSW 
will be applied to create a new biocomposite material.

In order to deliver on the above, the specific objectives  that will be fulfilled over the course of URBIOFIN are discussed below: 
Table 1.1.b URBIOFIN objectives and performance indicators based on milestones 
Objective 1. To evaluate the heterogeneity and comp ositional variability of the OFMSW for the design o f the 
biorefinery and select representative feedstock (linked to WP1) 
The main challenge to use the organic fraction of M SW as feedstock for bio-based industries lies in it s high complexity and 
variability in composition. The three main factors affecting this variability are the type of waste co llection system, the 
geographic location and seasonality. In URBIOFIN pr oject, the biorefinery processes will be designed a nd adapted taking 
into account this non-homogeneous feedstock and its  compositional variability, as well as the presence  of inhibitors to 
downstream biotechnological processes in order to a ssure its industrial feasible conversion into bio-b ased products. 
Representative MSW samples will be studied and selected for the biorefinery demonstration activities. 
Performance indicator : Milestone 1  
Objective 2. To define the industrial requirements in terms of final bioproducts properties (Linked to WP1) 
It will be defined in detail the requisites (purity , technical properties of biopolymers, N and C cont ent of biofertilisers, 
legislative requirements, etc.) that the bioproducts developed must fulfil to satisfy the requirements  of final users in order to 
facilitate future commercialisation. 
Performance indicator : Milestone 2 
Objective 3. To reduce the operational costs of the bioethanol production from OFMSW by 20% (linked to WP2)  
Some of the bioethanol pilot plant modules (pre-tre atment, simultaneous fermentation and sacharificati on modules, 
bioethanol separation units) will be adapted to the  new feedstock conditions in order increase the bio ethanol yield to be 
used as a chemical building block for bioethylene p roduction. As one of its goals for the adaptation o f these modules is to 
reduce 10% the energy costs of the plant. New monitoring technologies will improve the performance of the plant control by 
obtaining relevant parameter information through ad vanced sensors and adding an intelligent plant cont rol platform. Novel 
customized enzymes cocktails for OFMSW developed by  NOVO partner will improve the liberation of fermen table sugars 
from 65 to 80%, while minimising the presence of inhibitors to downstream processes.  
With the implementation of all these actions it is aimed to reduce the current state-of-the-art operational costs for bioethanol 
production from OFMSW by 20%.  
Performance indicator : Milestone 3 
Objective 4. To demonstrate at semi-industrial scal e the viability for the continuous conversion of bi oethanol 
produced from the OFMSW into bioethylene (linked to WP2) 
The continuous catalytic conversion of bioethanol f rom OFMSW into bioethylene will be demonstrated at semi-industrial-
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 7 
 
 
scale. The process will be evaluated in technologic al terms to certificate industrial feasibility. Tar get yield is up to 99% for 
bioethylene. Bioethylene produced will be in compli ance with international standards and market regula tions, and with 
comparable costs to comparable existing products to ensure future commercialisation. 
Performance indicator:  Milestone 4 
Objective 5. To demonstrate at semi-industrial scal e the continuous VFA production from partial AD of OFMSW 
(WP3) 
Demo-scale prototype for two phase AD system integr ating hydrolytic and Methanogenic reactor will be d eveloped in the 
project to treat OFMSW. At least 20g of VFA/L should be achieved in the first stage and degradation of 70% of soluble COD 
in the Methanogenic step is expected in the project. 
Performance indicator : Milestone 6 
Objective 6. To validate at semi-industrial scale at least 50-60% of operational yields for MCFA and PHA production 
at semi-industrial scale (WP3) 
Demo-scale prototype for production scl-PHA and mcl -PHA will be validated. On the one hand directly fr om VFA from AD 
throughout two processes of enrichment of biomass and use this biomass to accumulate scl-PHA using always mix culture, 
and in the other hand throughout elongation of VFA to MCFA with Cryptococcus and the same two processes of enrichment 
with VFA for produce biomass and accumulation with pure culture. The expected yields will be around 40 -50% for scl-PHA 
of biomass containing PHA and higher values (50-70%) for mcl-PHA. The final amount of both PHA types will be around 0.5 
tonnes per year. 
 Performance indicator : Milestones 7 and 8  
Objective 7. To validate at semi-industrial scale PHA extraction process (WP3) 
PHA extraction based on combination of mechanical d isruption with bead mills and enzymatic treatment w ill be applied to 
extract the PHA coming from scl-PHA (from VFA or bi omethane) or for mcl-PHA. In all the cases the obje ctive will be 
recover at least 60% of the accumulated PHA with good properties for further applications.  
Performance indicator : Milestone 9 
Objective 8. To validate at semi-industrial scale biogas upgrading using microalgae (WP4) 
Technological prototype for integral biogas upgradi ng, which combines the conversion of a MSW by-produ ct into a 
consumer product (i.e. biogas into biomethane), the  treatment of a waste effluent from anaerobic diges tion (digestate is 
used as nutrient source and thus carbon and phosphorous contents are reduced by > 70%, with nitrogen reductions > 90%) 
and the production of a microalgae biomass (biomass  productivities of 11-13 g m -2d-1) for further revalorization as 
biofertiliser. The final biomethane will comply with current legislation (CH 4>95%, CO 2<2.5%, H 2S<5 mg S m -3, O 2<1%) to be 
used as a consumer product for natural gas grid injection or as vehicle fuel. 
Performance indicator:  Milestone 10 
Objective 9. To produce new bio-based material coming from biogas (WP4) 
The novel two-stage process will be able to complet ely remove the H 2S and ~90-95% of the CH 4 contained in the biogas, 
producing ~0.7 g biomass per g of CH 4 treated. Under optimum operating conditions, the m ethanotrophic community will 
achieve a PHA accumulation capacity over 60% (w/w).  
Performance indicator:  Milestone 11.  
Objective 10. To produce final marketable products from the bio-based chemicals, polymers and additive s 
obtained in URBIOFIN biorefinery  and validate their performance (linked to WP5) 
In order to validate the whole value chain the biop roducts obtained in URBIOFIN biorefinery will be ut ilized by possible end 
users. Bioethylene will be used as ripening gas in post-harvest fruit chambers. PHA polymers will be u sed to produce 
plastic bags and agriculture plastic films, as well  as cosmetic packaging. Dry granules fermented-dige sted solid fraction of 
OFMSW and aminoacids from microalgae will be tested  as biofertiliser. Biomethane will be used directly  as end-product or 
as PHA precursor depending on business plan (differ ent countries have different prices for biomethane that can 
compensate or not going to other products). The per formance of all these developed final products will  be validated to 
demonstrate that they satisfy their expected functions. 
Performance indicator : Milestones 13, 14 and 15 
Objective 11. To demonstrate the technical, environ mental and economic viability of the developed proc esses and 
products in URBIOFIN biorefinery as well as regulatory compliance (WP6) 
Life cycle assessment (LCA) and life cycle cost (LC C) along with techno-economic assessment (TEA) will be performed on 
the processes developed for the selected bioproducts in order to prove the reduction of environmental and economic impact 
vs. current traditional materials and processes. Issue s regarding health risks and regulatory assessment will be studied, 
analysed and addressed during the project with the aim to ensure the compliance of the processes and p roducts from 
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 8 
 
 
URBIOFIN project. Techno-economic viability of the biorefinery with inputs from previous WPs will be e valuated and 
optimised. 
Performance indicator : Milestones 16, 17 and 18 
Objective 12. To deliver the impacts of the URBIOFI N project for enabled innovation in the European bi obased 
industries (WP7) 
Exploitation plans for sustaining the URBIOFIN conc ept beyond the project period will be created, wher eby the vision is to 
be the key driver of the bioeconomy innovation in E urope. This vision will be supported by a strong di ssemination strategy 
to raise awareness about bioproducts derived from O FMSW among the bio-based industries and the general  public. 
Business plans will be developed for the developed bioproducts to facilitate their market entry. Effec tive knowledge 
management activities will maximise IP potential. Engagement with Waste Management Authorities and Bio-based industry 
clusters will be promoted. 
Performance indicator : Milestones 19, 20 and 21.
1.2  Relation to the work programme   
The URBIOFIN project relates to the topic BBI 2016.D6 : Valorisation of the organic content of Municipal So lid Waste and 
contributing to the renewable circular economy . In particular the project addresses one of the BB I 2016 priorities, which is 
the “Development of biorefinery technologies and ef ficient processes to increase the competitive posit ion of the bio-based 
industries in Europe” (Scientific Priority 2). This  includes the implementation of technologies to ena ble the processing of a 
variety of biomass feedstock, the optimisation of p roduction routes for 2 nd  generation bio-based chemicals and intermediates i n 
order to effectively replace 1 st  generation sugars and the optimisation of bioproce sses, including bio-catalysis operations, by 
improving yields and cost competitiveness as compared to state-of-the-art. 
In order to effectively respond to the identified c hallenge we have mobilised a strong industry-academ ia partnership that 
integrates the required technologies and skills. Ta ble 1.2a provides a summary of how URBIOFIN address es the specific 
challenge and scope of BBI 2016.D6 topic. 
Table 1.2a  How URBIOFIN addresses the scope and challenges of the BBI 2016.D6 topic 
Challenges of BBI 2016.D6 How URBIOFIN addresses these challenges

deployment of treatment and conversion technologies  of the 
organic fraction of MSW into final or intermediate products 
for identified further use

products in the integration process.

proof of economic and industrial feasibility for th e proposed technology at 10 
tonnes per day. Addressed in WP6 integration

biotechnological processes

ensure market uptake, and in any case at least comp arable 
with existing comparable products. An accompanying full 
cost/benefit analysis takes into account the saving s 
associated with avoidance of disposing of waste. 
A sound business model is provided in the point 3.1 .2 in order to ensure 
market uptake. An accompanying full Life Cycle Cost  (LCC) analysis takes 
into account the savings associated with avoidance of disposing of waste will 
be performance at demonstration scale. LCC will be carried out on the 
processes developed for the selected bioproducts to  reduce economic 
impact vs. traditional materials. The economic viab ility of the biorefinery will 
be performed in WP6-7 with inputs from all previous  WPs. Addressed in 
WP6-7 
Safety, quality and purity of the products must be validated in 
order to meet commercial and/or regulatory requirem ents by 
actively building upon existing knowledge and 
standardisation activities. 
Monitoring and control systems integration will be identified in order to fulfil 
the requisites of final products: safety, quality a nd purity. The definition of the 
requisites needed to be fulfilled by the new bio-pr oducts according to the end 
user requirements and the EU legislation will be ma de and analysis to fulfil 
the standards are included as addressed Milestones 2.3 and 4.1. Addressed 
in WP1  
Proposals should also include a Life Cycle Assessme nt 
(LCA) in order to evaluate the environmental and so cio-
economic performance of the whole value chain. 
In WP6 environmental and socio-economic assessments  for the different 
product lines will be included. The results of the analysis will show that the 
costs of the bioproducts developed in URBIOFIN will  be comparable with the 
existing comparable products, while providing envir onmental and social-
economic advantages. Addressed in WP6
1.3  Concept and approach 
1.3.1  Overall concept underpinning the project 
The biorefinery concept for OFMSW aims at maximizin g the recovery of more diverse resources from MSW i n addition to 
bioenergy, by simultaneous exploitation and remedia tion of waste. The urban biorefinery will be compos ed of three inter-
related main modules  where each value chain will be developed (Figure 1 .3a). These modules will be demonstrated in three 
sites, in order to make use of already existing dem o facilities. Additional investment will be done to  these facilities to improve 
their efficiency and reach the targeted final bioproducts with the quality required for future market access. 
Module 1  will be located in Alcudia (Valencia, Spain), in t he biotechnological demonstration plant of IMECAL p roject 
coordinator. IMECAL together with the partner CIEMA T have developed a biotechnological patented techno logy (European 
Patent EPO 2112226, USA Patent 8399228 B2 and inter national PCT WIPO 099038) to produce second generation bioethanol 
from the OFMSW (PERSEO bioethanol ®). The plant has  a capacity to process 25 tonnes per day of OFMSW. The necessary 
facilities to use the bioethanol produced as buildi ng block for the production of bioethylene will be constructed and integrated in 
IMECAL demonstration plant. 
Module 2 will be located in Zaragoza (Spain) and Puertollano (Ciudad Real, Spain), in the MSW Technology Innovation Center 
“Alfonso Maíllo” owned by URBASER partner and in th e Biorefinery pilot plant of IRIAF. Specifically th e 2 AD systems as well 
as the biogas line will be placed in Zaragoza and the downstream line for VFA valorisation to PHA will be placed in Puertollano. 
The final extraction step will be performed in Wage ningen, since WR resources to perform this task, av oiding important 
investment costs to the process. 
Module 3  will be located in Zaragoza (Spain) to convert the biogas to biomethane with microalgae as well as validate its use as 
fuel for vehicles, and also its use for PHA production. Additionally in this module the biofertilisers uses will be evaluated against 
controls to verify the improved properties of these products.

Figure 1.3a  URBIOFIN modules 
Partners involved in URBIOFIN have large experience  in the required technologies and materials develop ed in the project as 
well as both national and international recognition (Section 4). The different modules of the project have as objective to produce 
high value products from the organic fraction of mu nicipal solid wastes, moving ahead from the typical  anaerobic and 
composting processes. For this reason the modules are thought to obtain high value products from 3 int ermediates, bioethanol, 
VFA, MCFA and biogas.  
In the last decade bioethanol has become the larges t biofuel with an estimated worldwide production of  150 billion litres in 
2020 
4. The rapid development of the bioethanol market ha s been accompanied by a growing interest in its use  as a renewable 
building block for the production of bio-based chemicals 5. In URBIOFIN project second generation bioethanol from OFMSW will 
be used as feedstock for the production of bioethyl ene, which is the raw material for the synthesis of  PE (the most demanding 
plastic in Europe, with more than 13 Mtones demande d in 2013) 6. Bioethanol will be produced from OFMSW by using s pecially 
developed PERSEO technology, which will be adapted to convert the bioalcohol, currently employed as a liquid biofuel, to 
produce bioethylene at semi-industrial scale. The r esidual solid which contain the components of MSW t hat are not fermented 
to produce bioethanol (proteins, lipids, pectin, et c.) is nowadays being used as solid biofuel. Howeve r, in URBIOFIN this 
material will be employed as feedstock stream for d ownstream valorisation in the biorefinery, incorpor ating it to Anaerobic 
Digestion (AD) in order to valorise this fermentation feedstock into valuable bioproducts.  
The current commercially established process to bio polymers production is mainly based on fossil fuel pathway, with clear 
disadvantages regarding non-renewable source as wel l as environmental impact. In order to reach high P HA yields the typical 
bacterial feedstock are costly sugars, which in thi s project is avoided. The relatively high costs are  the main reason which 
hinders biopolymers adoption in the market. The major advantage of this project lies at the reduction of costs using as substrate 
residual and renewable source non food competing su ch as VFA from AD and MCFA. It provides a very conv enient option to 
decrease the production costs of biopolymers. Moreo ver, the biochemical pathways proposed in this proj ect also offers a great 
advantage in terms of process flexibility, since yo u can use directly the VFA to produce scl-PHA for a griculture or low cost 
applications or you can apply the MCFA as substrate for accumulation PHA as mcl-PHA for high cost applications.

chemicals? Biofuels, Bioproducts and Biorefining (5), 486–494.

745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 11 
 
 
The symbiotic activity between photosynthetic micro organisms (microalgae and/or cyanobacteria) and het erotrophic and 
chemoautotrophic bacterial communities in photobior eactors operated at high pH constitutes a low-cost and environmentally 
friendly technological platform for the simultaneou s removal of CO 2, H 2S, NH 3 and VOCs from biogas. In this process, 
photosynthetic microorganisms employ the incident s olar light to fix the CO 2 contained in the biogas as biomass, with the 
subsequent production of O 2.  This O 2 generated in-situ in the photobioreactor is used b y chemoautotrophic bacteria to oxidize 
H2S and NH 3 to SO 42- and NO 3-, respectively. The O 2 is also employed for the oxidation of VOCs to CO 2 and H 2O as main end 
products. This oxygen demand for H 2S, NH 3 and VOCs oxidation avoids desorption of the photosynthetic oxygen present in the 
cultivation broth and therefore the contamination o f the upgraded biomethane with O 2. Further revalorization of the produced 
microalgae-bacterial biomass is possible via biofer tiliser production. A final biomethane polishing st ep for volatile methyl 
siloxanes removal will be also implemented. The rec ently demonstrated ability of specific microbial co mmunities for siloxanes 
biodegradation opens a new opportunity to biologica lly remove these contaminants commonly present in r aw biogas in a more 
environmentally friendly and less energy-intensive way. Their low solubility requires an additional ef fort to overcome mass 
transfer limitations when implementing this process  in conventional bioreactors, which will be address ed by innovative 
strategies for gas-liquid transport intensification . Microalgae from the photo bioreactor used for bio methane upgrading can be 
used, once dried as component for solid biofertilisers formulations and/or used for extraction of its aminoacids (thanks to its high 
content in proteins) and other active molecules wit h agronomic properties for the formulation of liqui d biofertilisers. The 
production of PHAs will be addressed in a two-stage  biological process consisting of a desulphurizatio n step followed by a 
PHAs production unit. Autotrophic denitrification constitutes a cost-efficient biogas purification method based on the injection of 
NO 3- as electron acceptor for H 2S oxidation. This technology constitutes a potentia l alternative to aerobic biofiltration (as it 
avoids external oxygen supply and mass transfer lim itations), and present clear advantages over their physical-chemical 
counterparts. Once pre-treated, the H 2S-free biogas will be supplemented to a high mass t ransfer bioreactor where type II 
methanotrophs able to accumulate PHAs will use the CH 4 contained in the biogas as carbon source. The high  CH 4 content and 
the CH 4/O 2 ratio support biogas as an ideal substrate for met hanotrophs growth and PHAs production. Different pr ocess and 
cultivating conditions will be tested in order to boost the PHAs accumulation capacity.  
The trans-disciplinary areas considered in this pro ject are: Chemical engineering, Biotechnology; Poly mer chemistry; 
Microbiology; Mathematical modelling and sustainabi lity assessment methodologies; Green chemistry; Eco nomics; Process 
control and monitoring and Plant engineering. 
1.3.2  Positioning of the project 
The technological developments of URBIOFIN have bee n positioned on the Technology Readiness Levels (TR Ls) scale (Table 
1.3a). The starting point is for the most part TRL5 -6 (Technology Validated-demonstrated in industrial ly relevant environment). 
The starting TRL is supported by the previous exper ience of the partners in the processes involved and  linked to a number of 
prior related projects. Technical and industrial kn owledge will be exchanged among the project partner s for obtaining the 
successful integration of technologies in industria l or operational environment with the participation  of end users, thereby 
reaching an overall TRL6-7 (Technology demonstrated  in industrially relevant environment-system protot ype demonstration in 
operational environment). For instance, the main st udies related to partial AD to produce VFAs are fro m last 5 years 
7. They 
mainly worked at lab-scale and using different resi dues trying to optimize the process parameters to e nhance acid production. 
Additionally, Kleerebezem et al.  8 (2015) indicated in a recent review that more work  is needed to demonstrate the possibilities 
of PHA production after partial AD against traditional biogas systems for multiple substrates.  
Besides, preliminary results obtained by the UVa in  the photosynthetic upgrading of biogas 9,10  together with previous studies 
described in literature shows the capacity of this system for the production of a biomethane complying  with the required 
composition for natural gas grid injection or use a s automotive fuel. The scale up potential of this p rocess has been 
demonstrated at laboratory scale in a controlled en vironment (TRL 4). Within this activity a system pr ototype will be operated 
and optimized in a real operation environment, demo nstrating the viability of the system for final app lications (TRL 7). The 
photosynthetic biogas upgrading is currently under evaluation in a 1.2 m 2 open pond located outdoors at the facilities of UV A 
under the frame of national R&D project. The succes sful results obtained so far guarantee a successful  scale up to the 300 m 2

large scale PHA-extraction from fermentation fluid/microbial biomass.

1.3.4  Overall approach and methodologhy 
Starting at TRL-4 or 5 as discussed in the previous  Section 1.3.2. Positioning of the project, the ove rall approach and 
methodology of URBIOFIN project is aimed to an adva ncement from TRL-5 to TRL-7. To reach such an end, the set of the 
project’s activities are categorised as shown in Figure 1.3a, and as further described in the points following.  
Modules for demo-scale process and integration: 
- Module 1  includes a demonstration plant for the conversion of OFMSW into second generation bioethanol using PE RSEO 
Bioethanol® technology patented by IMECAL and CIEMA T partners. This technological approach is based on  an enzymatic 
process with a moderate pre-treatment developed for  OFMSW as raw material, which avoids the presence o f inhibitors to the 
fermentation process. PERSEO Bioethanol® demonstrat ion plant in Alcudia is equipped with all the react ors, fermenters, and 
separation and distillation units needed for transf orming the OFMSW to bioethanol. After that, the pro duced bioethanol will be 
converted to Ethylene by an ‘Ethanol to Ethylene’ ( EtoE) demonstration plant. For the catalytic conver sion of bioethanol into 
bioethylene a new module will be incorporated downstream in the Alcudia demo plant. The bioethylene production unit will be a 
modular demonstration unit which contains all neces sary equipment to achieve a high yield and proper q uality of ethylene. The 
engineering study for the integration of these new modules into the existing bioethanol production facilities will be performed by 
G.I. Dynamics and adapted to the demonstration capa cities of PERSEO bioethanol plant. Finally the bioe thylene produced will 
be validated as ripening agent in post harvest fruit cambers (WP5). 
- Module 2  will be design and construct as an integrated demo -scale plant for the valorisation of the organic fr action of 
municipal solid wastes (OFMSW) to high-value produc ts, such as mcl-PHA and chemical building blocks as  biogas. The 
demonstration plant at the R&D Center of URBASER in  Zaragoza will include two-phase AD unit of 100 and  200 m 
3 
respectively, aimed to obtain volatile fatty acids (VFAs) in the first stage and also biogas as chemic al building block for module 
in the second stage. The bioethanol produced in mod ule 1 will be also used for elongation of VFA to me dium chain fatty acids 
(MCFA) and the use of the MCFA as carbon-source for  PHA accumulation as medium chain length polyhydrox yalcanoates 
(mcl-PHA products). A scale-up from lab to 100L fer menters will be performed using Cryptococcus  and similar other yeasts in 
the process. As a result, effluent coming from the hydrolytic reactor will be sent to Puertollano at I RIAF pilot plant where the 
demo plant for microbial elongation will be operate d with real effluent by IRIAF partner under the sup ervision of WR in order to 
optimize the process and trying to reach a high con version of VFA conversion to MCFA. This MCFA can be  used as final 
product for cosmetic application and also as substr ate for mcl-PHA production in the next task. IRIAF,  Ainia and WR will 
validate and operate the demo-scale plant for scl a nd mcl-PHA production that will be also placed in P uertollano to operate in 
batch mode. This will consist of two reactors (from  0.3 to 20 m 3), one for biomass production and other for PHA acc umulation. 
The first reactor will operate with the effluent of  hydrolytic reactor with pure culture (using Pseudomonas Putida)  and with mix 
culture, alternatively. The second reactor will be fed with the biomass of the first reactor and with MCFA for pure culture and 
also with VFA for mix culture to accumulate mcl-PHA and scl-PHA, respectively.  Finally, a comprehensive data analysis will be 
performed in order to evaluate and define the best parameters for the whole value chain to scl-PHA and mcl-PHA production at 
demo-scale level. PHA coming from the latter process will be extracted from biomass by a combination of mechanical treatment 
(Bead Mill) and enzymatic treatment (protease, lyso zyme, SDS/EDTA). This will be done in WR installati ons in collaboration 
with IRIAF and NATURE PLAST. The use of the extract ed mcl-PHA for cosmetic bioplastics production and MCFA as cosmetic 
component will be evaluated by NATRUE as end user. The use of the extracted scl-PHA will be evaluated by SES as end user 
for agricultural bioplastics.

rate algal pond photobioreactor interconnected to a n external absorption unit that will be constructed  and operated at the R&D

biopolymers (PHAs) using a high mass transfer unit.  
The three modules described above will be integrate d in one whole process which includes the three pro duct lines. The 
integration process in WP5 is aimed to prove the sc aling-up and the technical (and later economic) fea sibility of the URBIOFIN 
concept in order to progress for a future commercialisation of the process. The outcomes will be used for the environmental and 
economic assessment of the project. 
1.3.5  Sex and gender analysis 
The technologies, processes and materials developed  in URBIOFIN are gender-independent. All URBIOFIN P artners have 
internal gender equality jobs creation and promotion policies. The consortium will foster the presence of women in the technical 
and management teams and decision board implemented  to govern URBIOFIN project. The coordinator of URB IOFIN is Ms. 
Coll. Project aims to contribute to these objective s for enhancing Gender Equality by involving numero us female researchers 
(see section 4) in the project and by promoting wom en researchers to play a leading role in work packa ges . Actually, WP1 
(Ms. Leticia Regueiro), WP2 (Ms. Caterina Coll), WP3 (Ms. Cristina Álvarez), WP5 (Ms. Inmaculada González), WP6 (Ms. 
Rocío Roldán) and WP8 (Ms. Caterina Coll)  are managed by female researchers, and as it is de scribed in section 4, near to 
50% of the staff researchers involved in the project are women. 
The Treaty on European Union, the European policy e stablishes equal opportunities between women and me n. In particular 
articles 2 and 3 establish equality between women a nd men as a specific task of the Community and also  to promote equality. 
Monitoring the progress toward gender equality in s cience has become a well-established activity of th e European Union 
research policy. In accordance with these principles, the approach of URBIOFIN is fully in line with D irective 2002/73/EC of the 
European Parliament and of the Council of 23 Septem ber 2002 on the implementation of the principle of equal treatment for 
men and women as regards access to employment, vocational training and promotion, and working conditions.
1.4  Ambition 
1.4.1  Advance of the project beyond the state-of-the art 
Below are described the ambitious advances brought by URBIOFIN development starting with a short summary of current state-
of-the-art and the proposed developments in the project. 
State of the art in the bioethanol production from the OFMSW  
The conventional fermentation process is a commercial success in the conversion of edible biomass (mainly sugar in Brazil and 
starch in the USA) to first generation bioethanol. However, due to the competition with food and conce rns about ecological 
systems, recent research has shifted towards bioeth anol production from non-food biomass feedstocks (l ignocellullosic 
materials from agricultural, industrial and urban w astes), which are low cost and abundantly available  sources. The growing 
market for bioethanol as a biofuel has also been ac companied by an increasingly interest as a useful p latform molecule for the 
production of value-added chemicals (some examples are: ethylene, propylene, 1,3-butadiene, acetaldehy de, diethyl ether, 
ethylene oxide, n-butanol, acetic acid and ethyl acetate) 16 .  
Second generation bioethanol from OFMSW can be prod uced by thermochemical and biochemical methods 17 . In thermal 
process, bioethanol is produced via gasification of  the feedstock at a high pressure and further catal ytic conversion of the 
                                                           
16  Weusthuis, R.A., Aarts, J.M.M.J.G., Sanders, J.P.M., 2011. From biofuel to bioproduct: is bioethanol a suitable fermentation feedstock for synthesis of 
bulk chemicals? Biofuels, Bioproducts and Biorefining (5), 486–494  
17  Progress in bioethanol processing. Balat M, Balat H and Oz C. Progress in Energy and Combustion Science, 2008, 34, 551-573.  
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 16 
 
 
syngas into bioethanol. In the biochemical pathway, the substrate is firstly pre-treated in order to make the cellulosic component 
accessible to the cellulases for the subsequent enz ymatic saccharification step. Sugars -mainly glucos e- are released after the 
enzymatic hydrolysis and are then fermented to prod uce bioethanol. The processes usually employed in t he fermentation of 
hydrolysate are simultaneous saccharification and f ermentation (SSF) and separate hydrolysis and ferme ntation (SHF). 
Conventionally, the SHF process has been employed b ut SSF is superior for ethanol production since it can improve ethanol 
yields by removing end product inhibition and eliminate the need for separate reactors, which is also cost effective 18 . 
Today, there are several technologies for the conve rsion of OFMSW into second generation bioethanol, a ll of them at 
demonstration scale. Some of these processes have been developed by Bluefire, Coskata, New Planet Energy or Fulcrum. 19  In 
these projects not only OFMSW is used as feedstock,  but also agroindustrial wastes, where it is pre-tr eated by severe and 
costly thermochemical processes. PERSEO Bioethanol® , patented by IMECAL and CIEMAT partners (European Patent EPO 
2112226, USA Patent 8399228 B2 and international PC T WIPO 099038) is developed and specialized exclusi vely to the 
bioconversion of OFMSW, and it is based on an enzym atic process with a moderate pre-treatment, which a voids the presence 
of inhibitors to the fermentation process. PERSEO B ioethanol® demonstration plant is equipped with all  the reactors, 
fermenters, and separation and distillation units n eeded for transforming the OFMSW to bioethanol. The  typical ethanol yields 
obtained vary from 100 – 130 L of bioethanol per to nne of organic waste in dry bases. The bioethanol p roduction involves 
several steps:  
A. The raw material is subjected to a pre-treatment wh ich aims to eliminate the inerts contained in the o rganic matter (stones, 
plastics, metals, glass, etc.).  
B. A thermochemical dilute acid hydrolysis of the raw material is done in order to produce a s olubilizing of the cellulose and 
hemicellulose contained in the MSW.  
C. After the feedstock is hydrolysed, a SSF process is done. The cellulose saccharification and ferment ation of glucose to 
ethanol is produced in the same fermenter, adding t he enzymes, yeast and nutrients suitable for effici ent conversion of sugars 
to ethanol.  
D. The fermentation sludge obtained is subjected to a distillation process in order to concentrate the ethanol produced in the  
fermentation of the OFMSW.  
E. Dehydration process is carried out of the mixture by passing through a molecular sieve to achieve dehydration required for 
ethanol (> 99.5%) as a final product.  
The main challenges of the biochemical route concer n the integration of the system which requires a co ordinated effort in 
improving each step to achieve an overall high effi ciency and a decreased production cost of bioethano l. The improvement of 
OFMSW pretreatment processes and the introduction of the latest biotechnological advances to decrease the operational costs 
(specifically those focused on the development on new generation of tailor made enzyme cocktails, which are able to effectively 
depolymerize cellulose and starch) have allowed to reduce considerably the price of MSW bioethanol produced.

sugars have been developed, from technical and econ omical point of view. URBIOFIN will use PERSEO Bioe thanol® 
technology to produce bioethanol from MSW to be use d as chemical building block, in order to improve t he operation process 
costs through the use of most cost-effective pretreatments, and tailor made enzyme cocktails supplied by NOVOZYMES partner 
for the specific process. New generation of enzyme cocktails will be used in URBIOFIN demonstration ac tivities. These new 
enzymes cocktails, targeting for OFMSW, increase the amount of liberated fermentable sugars and, as a consequence, the final 
bioethanol yield, which improves the process econom y. Moreover the bioethanol produced from the OFMSW will be employed 
as a chemical building block for the production of bioethylene, which is a higher added value application than the current use as

State of the art in the use of bioethanol as building block for the production of bioethylene 
 
The rapid development of the bioethanol market in t he last decade has been accompanied by a growing in terest in its use as 
renewable feedstock for the manufacture of bio-base d chemicals 20 . Moreover, bioethanol was recently identified as o ne of the 
potential top bio-based raw materials for the chemical industry 21 . Important technological advances have also been made in the 
catalytic conversion of bioethanol (e.g. dehydrogen ation, reforming, dehydratation, oxidation, etc.) a nd many significant 
chemicals have been successfully synthesized: ethyl ene, propylene, diethyl ether, 1,3-butadiene, aceta ldehyde, ethyl acetate, 
etc 22 . Given the large number of options for bioethanol conversion Posada and coworkers 23  identified the most promising 
derivatives from a sustainability point of view (co vering economic, environmental and health and safet y aspects), being 
ethylene  one of the most favourable compounds. The authors identified the most promising bioethanol derivative s by 
comparing the bioethanol-based conversion routes to its respective petrochemical counterparts.  
Nowadays, most of the global production of ethylene  comes from the petrochemical industry by steam cra cking of naphta. 
However, the catalytic conversion of bioethanol to ethylene is a mature technology with molar yields r eaching above 99% 24 .    
There are a lot of industrial examples, most of the m in Asia with capacities varying from 40 KTA to 30 0 KTA. Most of these 
plants producing bioethylene use first generation b ioethanol as feedstock (corn, starch, sugarcane). S ince 2010 the Brazilian 
company Braskem commercializes “I'm greenTM Polyeth ylene”, a bioPE produced from ethanol sugarcane, ha ving an annual 
production capacity of 200,000 tonnes25 . 
Beyond the state of the art in URBIOFIN project bio ethylene will be produced at demonstration scale fr om the catalytic 
conversion of second generation bioethanol coming f rom OFMSW. This bioethylene will be used as a ripen ing gas in post-
harvest fruit chambers. Moreover, it could also be polymerized to produce a more sustainable and econo mically competitive 
bio-polyethylene compared to commercial bio-PE from sugarcane.  
State of the art in the production of VFA from anaerobic digestion of OFMSW  
The objective of the partial anaerobic digestion (A D), i.e two-phase AD, consist into induce the VFA a ccumulation, separating 
the hydrolytic phase from the methanogenic step which has been validated for multiple wastes, such as municipal solid waste 26 . 
To promote this biological VFAs production, maximiz ing the product concentration, but also controlling  the product spectrum 
(type of individual VFAs produced) several paramete rs should be taken into account (i.e. hydraulic ret ention time, pH, 
temperature, etc.). The main drawback of this techn ology, even optimized to produce high VFAs concentr ations, is that the 
VFAs mixture has no clear economical value. The spe cific VFAs have to be purified from the liquid effl uent to be 
commercialized as pure acids. However the purificat ion methods, such as precipitation, distillation or  electrodialysis imply high 
cost in the process.

State of the art in the production of PHA biopolymer from VFA 
 
Medium-chain fatty acids ( i.e., from C6 to C22) are more valuable than short-chain  fatty acids (SCFA, e.g ., acetic, propionic, 
butyric acid) from an economical point of view. SCF As are major products in the first stage of anaerob ic fermentation, and only 
feeding ethanol to this effluent, MCFA formation is  enhanced through chain elongation. Anaerobic micro organisms, such as 
Clostridium kluyveri  which are already present in the anaerobic ferment ors leading to VFA are known to elongate short-chai n 
acids to primarily C6 and C8 by combining them with  ethanol. In order to maximize MCFA production, maj or parameters 
affecting the process are ethanol concentration and  temperature. For production of even longer MCFA’s,  such as C10 – C18 a 
separate fermentation using dedicated microorganism s such as Cryptococcus  is proposed. By applying specific fermentation 
conditions and specific variants of this yeast, dif ferent product ranges for MCFA’s can be achieved su ch as C8-C12 or C14-
C18. Another advantages of the elongation process i n this project is related to the use of residual bi o-ethanol coming from 
OFMSW undergoing a hydrolysis and fermentation step  combined, known as simultaneous sacharification an d fermentation 
(SSF), previously explained. This residual stream m ight be directly used for chain elongation, avoidin g additional cost due to 
reagents purchase. The main disadvantage of the elo ngation process is the lower MCFA production rates.  For this reason in 
this project the objective will be used a reactor c onfiguration with biomass retention to favour the e nrichment of MCFA 
producers and avoids the longer start-up, despite t he additional cost of the carrier material 27 . The different MCFAs will also be 
used as a substrate to polyhydroxyalcanoates (PHA) producing microorganisms. Normally the short VFA ar e used as direct 
feeding for PHA accumulating bacteria, however in t his the mixed PHA-producing culture and a pure cult ure of Pseudomonas 
putida will be fed with the MCFA’s generated in the elonga tion process. However medium-chain PHAs (mcl-PHA) a ppear to be 
much more elastomeric and resistant, and they posse ss attractive properties such as low melting points , high elasticity, and 
biodegradability, respect to SVFA-PHA, offering higher value applications 28,29 .

State of the art in biogas upgrading by microalgae photobioreactor 
 
Biogas is the byproduct from anaerobic digestion wi th highest valorization potential, with an expected  production in Europe in 
2030 of 20 billion Nm 3/year according to the Biogas European Association.  Due to its high CH 4 content, biogas constitutes a 
suitable raw material for the production, via upgra ding, of a biofuel similar to natural gas: biomethane . Nonetheless, biogas 
relatively high production cost, the lack of tax in centives and the inherent presence of pollutants su ch as CO 2, H 2S, NH 3, VOCs 
and volatile methyl siloxanes, have limited the use  of biomethane for injection in natural gas grids o r as automotive fuel. In this 
sense, the high production, upgrading, compression and distribution costs results in biomethane prices  ~0.6 € Nm -3, 
considerably higher than those of natural gas (~0.4  € Nm -3) 30,31 . It is therefore clear that a reduction in the cos t of biogas 
upgrading based on technological advances will incr ease the competitiveness of this bioresource. Curre nt scenario for biogas 
upgrading is mainly based on stand-alone physical-c hemical technologies capable of removing either CO 2 or H 2S. CO 2 
abatement is performed by highly energy-intensive t echnologies (0.25-0.3 kWh Nm -3) which entail high inversion costs (1,500-
6,000 € (Nm 3/h) -1), such as chemical scrubbers, pressure swing adsor ption or cryogenic separation 32 . Similarly, physical-
chemical technologies for H 2S removal involve prohibitively high operating cost s and an excessive carbon footprint 33 . New 
developing biotechnologies for biogas upgrading, al though more cost-efficient and environmentally frie ndly than their physical-
chemical counterparts, still present important draw backs which limit their full-scale implementation. For instance, CO 2 archaeal 
bioconversion by H 2 injection in digesters presents limited applicability while posing explosion risks, and H 2S biological oxidation 
by air or O 2 injection entails operational problems related wit h elemental sulphur accumulation or biomethane cont amination 
with O 234 . Thus, despite the promising resource recovery pot ential of biogas, there is no available technology in the market 
(either physical-chemical or biological) able to si multaneously remove CO 2, H 2S, NH 3, VOCs and siloxanes from biogas in an 
efficient, economically feasible and environmentall y sustainable way, and capable of producing in a si ngle stage a biomethane 
with enough quality for its injection in the natura l gas grid or its use as automotive fuel 35 . One of the proposed technologies for 
biogas revalorization relies on the symbiotic activ ity of algae and bacteria in photobioreactors, whic h can support the 
simultaneous removal of CO 2 and H 2S in a single process 36 . Other biogas contaminants, such as NH 3 or VOCs, will be also 
likely oxidized in the process. This innovative pro cess has been successfully implemented in UVA labor atory in open 
photobioreactors interconnected to an external abso rption column to promote CO 2 and H 2S gas-liquid mass transfer from the

raw biogas to the cultivation media (Figure 1.4d). Preliminary results achieved CO 2 and H 2S removals over 95% and 100% 
respectively, and further optimization of the opera ting parameters resulted in a biomethane compositio n complying with most 
European legislations 37,38 . This system thus presents a high potential for sc ale-up, offering a promising upgrading capacity

The presence of volatile methyl siloxanes (VMS) has  also consistently limited the energy use of biogas  due to their detrimental 
effect on the equipment and piping of the process. Current physical-chemical processes for VMS abateme nt (such as 
adsorption, absorption or condensation) entail proh ibitive investment and operating costs 39 . The development of low-cost 
biological alternatives able to efficiently remove VMS by combining high-mass transfer units together with highly efficient 
microorganisms will be also addressed in this proje ct. A novel final polishing stage to be implemented  after the upgrading 
process will be thus obtained. Revalorization of bi ogas via bioconversion into PHAs (biocompatible and  biodegradable 
biopolymers) constitutes another attractive alternative due to their high market value (1.5 – 5 € kg -1PHA ). In this sense, H 2S-free 
biogas obtained via anoxic biofiltration, is an ideal substrate for cultivating type II methanotrophs able to accumulate PHAs as a 
result of its high CH 4 content, the high ratio CH 4/O 2 and the low cost associated to this raw material. P revious literature studies 
and the experience of the UVA research group on PHA s production from diluted CH 4 emissions support the potential of this 
valorization strategy 40 . This process will be implemented in high mass tra nsfer bioreactors, able to remove the H 2S present in 
the biogas by NO 3- injection and further convert the CH 4 into PHAs.

Beyond the state of the art, URBIOFIN will validate  two technological platforms for raw biogas revalor ization at demonstration

State of the art in the production of bio-fertilisers from microalgae and OFMSW 
 
Biofertilisers and Biostimulants could be defined a s “Probiotics” for plants: Products bringing out be nefits from interactions 
between microorganisms – plants – environment. They  are newly produced and are not regulated in all th e EU member states. 
The new (revised) Fertiliser regulation that is in course foresees an EU harmonised frame for Bioferti lisers and Biostimulants . 
“Roadmap for the Revision of the Fertilisers Regula tion (EC) No 2003/2003” indicates that “...,  both producing industries and 
Member States are interested in extending the scope  of Regulation (EC) 2003/2003 to also cover organic  fertilisers, organo-
mineral fertilisers, soil improvers, growing media,  agronomic fertiliser additives and possibly plant biostimulants to achieve full 
harmonisation of the legislation for the entire sector....”.

Regarding the  Regulatory frame for biofertilisers in Europe  the situation is confusing an there is no referenc e in the available 
revised Fertiliser Regulation draft of the inclusion of products with living microorganisms under the chapter of Biostimulants. Lot 
of work and efforts must be done in the next years to not let loose the resources and markets that are  being developed by 
European companies in the EU territory and worldwide. 
The important aspects to take into account for fertiliser are: 
- Classification of biofertilisers according its primary agronomical effects  
- Ingredients of a biofertiliser  
- How to formulate biofertilisers?: depend on the objective and the application conditions  
• Solid state 
• Liquid state (for fertirrigation, nebulisation, etc) 
- Compost, soil improvers and growing media and the biofertilisers  
Different scientific works and field trials previou sly performed by the members of the consortium have  indicated the interest of 
combining rich C substrates like compost (biologica lly rich) or biochar (biologically inert material) together and with selected 
microorganisms strains or its cocktails for great b iostimulant, fortifier and/or plant protection effe cts. One problem to be solved 
when combining biofertilisers or microorganisms with compost, soil improvers and growing media (and digestate and biochar) is 
the competition and interactions of selected strain s and the natural populations in the organic substr ate. Other problems could 
be the substrate possible toxicity and adequacy of the nutrient profile, and also excess temperatures in the substrate (over 
35ºC) can cause survival or functionality problems. 
The advances proposed in URBIOFIN beyond the state of the art are to produce specific solid biofertili ser combining the solid 
fraction from the second anaerobic digestion with s pecific co-substrates as well as liquid biofertilis er with high aminoacids 
content coming from microalgae hydrolysis to be app lied in mixtures with several microorganisms or add itives to make 
competing products in the market with high added value that will be validated against controls to verify good properties.

2.1.2  Improvement of innovation capacity and integration of new knowledge 
URBIOFIN will demonstrate at semi-industrial scale the OFMSW valorisation for the production of higher  value and marketable 
products rather than conventional MSW processing (i ncineration for energy recovery o composting). The valorisation of 
OFMSW as a sustainable raw material creates an impo rtant competitiveness opportunity for the European bioeconomy and will 
increase the innovation capacity of our industry. I t is expected that the industrial biotechnology mar ket sector in Europe almost 
double by 2050, compared to 2013 values, reaching u p to 50 billion € 41 . Bioethanol, bio based plastics and aminoacids (al l of 
them target bioproducts obtained in URBIOFIN propos ed biorefinery) are expected to be the top three pr oducts with higher 
growth (Figure 2.1a). In EU28 2013, the chemicals and plastics sector represented a turnover of 48 billion € and 160,000 jobs 42 . 
Initiatives such as URBIOFIN will enable industrial innovation in many different sectors to convert OF MSW as a raw material to 
produce bio based products with countless final app lications. Our approach will significantly contribu te to the bio-based 
industries ambitious target aiming to replace 30 % of the oil based chemical and materials in Europe b y 2030 43 , while at the 
same time helping to solve a great societal and environmental problem which is the MSW management.  
 
Figure 2.1a  Industrial biotechnology market in Europe 44  
The strong industrial composition of URBIOFIN conso rtium (7 SMEs and 2 large companies) together with research institutions 
(2 universities, 3 RTOs) and 2 clusters will enhance the innovative potential of the project. Industrial partners are integrated into 
the project decision making structure to ensure tha t the involved industrial sectors drive project act ivities and benefit from the 
project results. URBIOFIN will validate the whole v alue chain with the direct participation of waste m anagement authorities and 
end-users of the developed bioproducts, which will guarantee high impact and effective dissemination a nd exploitation of the 
results. High economic impact will be achieved through strengthening the competitiveness of the industrial partners in the target 
market sectors and related ones by: 1) promoting the use of URBIOFIN  products, components  and systems  in high value-added 
applications while keeping costs competitive, 2) st rengthening and differentiating the EU Industry, 3)  increasing workers’ skills. 
Detailed information of the market for each developed bioproducts  in URBIOFIN (bioethanol, MCFAs, biomethane, as 
building blocks and bioethylene, PHA, biocomposites , solid and liquid biofertilisers as final products ) is presented in the 
following pages. 
Bioethanol 
: Main current use of bioethanol is as transport biofuel or fuel additive for gasoline. Bioethanol has also been used in 
the beverage industry and as renewable chemical bui lding block for the production of ethylene, propyle ne, diethyl ether, 1,3-
butadiene, acetaldehyde, ethyl acetate, etc. 45 (Figure 2.1b). Global bioethanol market volume was 100 billion litres in 2014 46 . 
However, it is expected a bioethanol production of 150 billion litres in 2020 47 , which means an increase of 50% compared to 
current market volumes. Currently bioethanol is pro duced from edible biomass, mainly from sugar in Bra zil and starch in USA 
(Figure 2.1c). However, due to the competition with  food and concerns about ecological systems, recent  research has shifted 
towards bioethanol production from non-food biomass  feedstocks (lignocellullosic materials from agricu ltural, industrial and 
urban wastes), which are low cost and abundantly av ailable sources. Second generation lignocellulosic ethanol is near 
commercialisation, with first-of-a-kind commercial plants built or currently under construction.

MCVFAs Medium-chain fatty acids with carbon chain lengths varying from 8 to 14 have a broad range of applicat ions as 
lubricants, creams, soaps, antimicrobials, and edib le oils. They are not constituents of fossil fuels and relative complicated 
chemical processes are needed to make them from longer-chain fatty acids. 
Caprylic acid is widely used in the cosmetic, food and feed industry for its antimicrobial activity. D ecanoic acid is applied in the 
chemical industry for its lubrication properties, b ut also in the cosmetic industry as lubricant but a lso because of its flavour 
properties. Lauric acid, as sodium salt, is soap an d as such is applied in most detergents, soaps and other cleansing agents 
used in the Home & Personal Care and Cosmetic indus try. It is also more-and -more applied as antimicrobial ingredient in Food 
& Feed Industry. Myristic acid is mostly applied in  the Cosmetic Industry. The total fatty acid market  size is expected to hit 
12,000 M€ in 2017, about 40% of this market in the personal care (detergent, soap, cosmetics) sector. The current market size 
for caprylic acid is approximately 2.5 M€. The mark et size for decanoic acid is approximately 50 M€, f or lauric acid 
approximately 2,000 M€ and for myristic acid approximately 600 M€. The fatty acid market size is expected to double in the next 
5-6 years in both the cosmetic and food applications.  
Biomethane : Biogas obtained as a by-product from the anaerobi c treatment of OMSW represents a renewable energy s ource 
based on its high CH 4 content. The actual European network of ~14,000 an aerobic digesters is expected to increase within th e 
next years in order to supply up to 18-20 million Nm 3 in 2030 according to the latest European Biogas Association’s estimations. 
In this context, the development of new biotechnolo gies for biogas revalorization via its upgrading to  biomethane will contribute 
to the EU objective of developing a new European bi o-economy less dependent on fossil fuels and based on the use of 
biological raw materials and bio-processing methods. For instance, one of the main direct uses of biom ethane is as automotive 
fuel, which can be applied with a similar compositi onal standard as pipeline natural gas. A very rough  estimation of current 
biomethane use worldwide for automotive purposes is  2-3 TWh/a, which is expected to rapidly increase u p to 6 TWh/a 
according to recent evaluations. However, and despi te the interest and potential competitiveness of bi omethane for fuel 
production, important limiting factors such as low economic efficiency or the low acceptance of the ac tual feedstock still 
challenge biomethane future prospects. URBIOFIN wil l develop a novel alternative to increase the compe titiveness of 
biomethane against other fossil fuels as natural ga s or diesel. First of all, the use of biogas from t he digestion of OMSW 
ensures the sustainability of the feedstock used fo r biomethane production. Moreover, the upgrading sy stem based on a 
biological photosynthetic process will significantl y reduce the actual market price of biomethane. In this sense, biomethane

actual cost amounts to ~0.6 € Nm -3, significantly higher than that of natural gas (about 0.4 € Nm -3), being the upgrading process 
one of the main contributors (~25-30%) to the total  cost. Finally, the use of biomethane to replace na tural gas can lead to a 
significant reduction of greenhouse gases (GHG) emi ssions, achieving savings of over 80% when compared  to fossil fuel 
alternatives. This will also contribute to the ambi tious international commitments of the EU regarding  reduction in GHG 
emissions together with the new goals of producing a clean energy. In summary, the supplementation of a suitable feedstock for  
biogas production and the upgrading and conditionin g to biomethane are key hotspots that will be addre ssed by URBIOFIN to 
ensure the economic and environmental sustainability of the final biomethane.  
Bioethylene:  Ethylene is primarily a monomer used as a feedstoc k in the manufacture of polymer plastics (PE, PET, PEG, 
PVC and PS), fibers and other organic chemicals tha t are ultimately consumed in the packaging, transpo rtation and 
construction industries and in a multitude of indus trial and consumer markets. Total worldwide product ion of ethylene was 127 
million tons in 2014, being one of the most consume d chemicals 50 . Polyethylene (PE), the largest ethylene derivativ e market, 
accounted for over 60% of global ethylene consumpti on in 2014 51 . During 2009–2014, world ethylene consumption grew  at an 
average rate of almost 4.5% per year, while capacit y increased at a slower rate of about 3.5%. Ethylen e consumption is 
expected to grow at about 4% per year over the next five years. Capacity additions will match this growth 52 . 
PHA : The high value of the PHA is based not only in the bioBased production process itself, but also in its  biodegradable 
nature, as seen in following picture (Figure 1). Bi obased polymers such as PHA are generally more sust ainable materials than 
their petrochemical-based counterparts,  and,  as  previously  mentioned,  they can  be  produced  fro m  a  wide  range  of  
renewable resources. The actual market segments of PHA consist in flexible packaging and mulch films ( used in blends), and 
some niche markets in consumer goods (cosmetic pack agings, horticulture pots, biodegradable cutlery, e tc.). The most 
representative PHAs are polyhydroxybutyrate (PHB) a nd polyhydroxyvalerate (PHV) and its copolymer (PHB V), with physical 
properties similar to polyethylene and polypropylen e. In both markets, besides its physical properties , PHA provides 
biodegradability. This is particularly adapted to t hese markets where the product shelf-life is low. I n particular in the case of 
mulch films, a major advantage arises in this case in addition to having the advantages of a tradition al plastic film: after use 
there is no need of collecting the film. In 2014, the global production capacities of bioplastics amounted to 1.7 million tons.  
 
Figure 2.1d  Production of PHA in 2014. 
This translates to approximately 680,000 hectares o f land. The key players in the PHA market are Bioma tera, Biomer, Bio-On 
SROL, Kaneka corporation, Meredian Ins., Metabolix Inc, Newlight Technologies, LIC, PHA industrial S.A ., Polyferm Canada 
Inc., Shenzhen Ecomann Biotech Co Ltd, Tianan Biolo gic Materials Co and Tianiin Greenbio Materials Co Ltd. PHA could be

Biocomposites : Composite materials as used e.g. in the construct ion, automotive and chemical industries, combine at tributes 
from two of more different materials in one combine d application. Examples are the combination of (bio )plastics with natural 
materials/fibers coming from wood, wheat or starch.  The composite materials are forming a rapidly grow ing market with a 
market value for WPC alone of 4000 M € per year. Th e total packaging market in Europe is approximately  130 billion €. Over 
50% of this market is biobased (paper, board, wood)  and 31% is plastic. The major plastic packaging ma terial is PE-based 
(56%) for mostly films, wraps, and bags but also so me bottles and containers (HD-PE) and smaller amoun ts of PP (19%), PET 
(11%), PS (10%) and PVC (4%) are used. For packagin g, very few composite materials are currently avail able. However the 
possibilities are extremely high since composites p otentially can combine the favourable attributes of  various compounds, e.g. 
the light weight and strength of plastic with the abundant availability and flexibility of paper and board.    
Fertilisers: Segment of solid biofertilisers is more competitive  with traditional mineral and organic-mineral ferti lisers, with 
greater size but lower value added. Regarding liqui d biofertilisers, microalgae grown on AD liquid and  gaseous streams will be 
used as protein source for aminoacids production vi a hydrolysis, to be used as main component of liqui d biofertilisers. 
Regarding liquid biofertilisers in general and base d in aminoacids and peptids in particular, are a fa st growing but smaller 
market but with much higher value added products, d esignated typically for fertirrigation and fruit an d vegetables intensive 
crops. The Market of biofertiliser in 2014 can be s een in picture 2.1f. The increasing awareness regar ding health and wellness, 
the growing organic food industry, and the significant increase in the price of chemicals have played a huge role in providing the 
necessary boost to the global biofertilizers indust ry. Global Biofertilizers Market is expected to rea ch USD 1.88 Billion by 2020 
at a CAGR of 14.0% from 2015 to 2020. The phosphate -solubilizing segment is the fastest-growing market  in the biofertilizers 
industry across the globe. 
The market was dominated by North America in 2014 and accounted for the largest share in the total biofertilisers market. Asia-
Pacific is projected to be the fastest-growing mark et for between 2015 and 2020 due to the increasing prices of chemical 
fertilizers and increasing food demand due to popul ation growth. In terms of type, the nitrogen fixing  biofertilisers segment is 
projected to be the largest market for biofertilisers; whereas in terms of crop-type, cereals & grains is projected to be the largest 
market for biofertilisers in 2014 (Figure 2.1g). Th e cyanobacteria segment is projected to be the larg est market for biofertilisers 
by 2020 in terms of microorganisms. Furthermore, by  application, seed treatment is projected to be the  largest market for 
biofertilisers in between 2015 and 2020. The biofertilisers market is characterized by intense competition due to the presence of 
a large number of both large and small-scale firms.  Expansions, acquisitions, agreements and new produ ct launches are the 
key strategies adopted by market players to ensure their growth in the market.

2.1.3  Other environmental and socially important impacts 
From a LCA perspective, the project concept provide s a very promising way to reduce the emissions of g reenhouse gases 
(CO 2) in terms of kg CO 2 abated per kg of OFMSW treated to Bioethanol, Ethy lene, biopolymers and additives, compared to 
other fossil-fuel-based alternatives. The exact amount of the reduction of the overall GWP will be cal culated as part of the LCA 
that will be carried out based on the results provi ded by the project, but there are several items ind icating a positive balance in 
terms of kg CO 2 abated per kg of OFMSW treated: 
1.  The project entails the production of new biobased products from OFMSW which can be considered alterna tive to similar 
fossil-fuels-based products. For example, the project proposes the production of high value microalgae amino acids on the base 
of N and CO 2 directly recovered from OFMSW, which is an alterna tive to the high carbon foot print protein crops us ing of 
synthesis fertilizers, 
2.  The CO 
2 generated during the biopolymers degradation is carbon neutral in contrast to equivalent fossil-fuel-based polymers, 
3.  The exhausted CO 2 from biofuel combustion is carbon neutral,  
4.  The system entails the production of renewable power sources such as the biogas, 
5.  The low energy intensity of this type of process co mpared to conventional management systems based on AD, composting 
and incineration technologies. Mixed culture PHA fr om industrial waste water has been shown to have a lower GWP than 
HDPE, assuming a high concentration or COD 54 . The results from Kendall 55 (for PHA from waste) show a lower non-renewable 
energy use than petrochemical plastics. 
6.  Finally, as the extraction of PHA will be carried o ut using non-toxic dissolvent (alkaline-acid soluti ons, green dissolvent), the 
conventional recovery operation (normally based on thermal distillation techniques) is not required.  
In addition to environmental importance, it is necessary consider social importance of the project. European waste management 
sector currently employs around 1 million people (s ource PSIRU) and the anaerobic digestion sector emp loys over 70,000 
people according to EBA (European Biogas Associatio n) but there is still a wide potential for a lot mo re employment growth 
through adequate treatment of organic fraction of M SW in order to obtain chemical building blocks, bio polymers and additives 
by implementing the proposed new bio-based value chains.  
Regarding direct job creation, highly qualified staff specialized in obtaining the new products will be necessary to carried out the 
proposed activities. Moreover, the URBIOFIN demonst ration activities are expected to create 18 highly qualified direct jobs 
among the project partners during the project. Afte r that, the future development of the project conce pt will allow transforming 
millions of tonnes of organic municipal waste into valuable products. Different companies from several  countries will be 
necessary to carry out the transformation from the obtained chemical building blocks or polymers to new bio-based products. As 
a result, not only operators or process biological experts but also sales personal will be required to  manage the output of the 
mentioned building blocks and raw materials in an international market. After the project, the new bio-based value chains and 
business will require the recruitment of 15-20 new technicians  in the involved companies to manage the production  of the 
bio-based products and their future commercialization.

There is also a huge potential to create new indirect jobs  related to the growth of new markets for these new  biobased 
products, their future commercialization and produc t distribution within the involved bio-based econom y clusters and final user 
industries of the European circular economy. 
Moreover, the project will contribute to the adopti on of new models for OFMSW management since it prov ides a competitive 
conversion of mentioned wastes to bioproducts. Ther efore, it is aligned with the EU Bioeconomy Strateg y which addresses the 
production of renewable biological resources and their conversion into vital products and bio-energy. It will contribute not only in 
scaling up of new technologies and processes for th e bioeconomy but also in developing markets and com petitiveness in 
bioeconomy sectors.  
The processes involved in the project will be inter connected so that a versatile and efficient biorefi nery for OFMSW will be 
demonstrated. Bio-based industries involving research and innovation focused on the sustainable use of organic waste 
for the production of bio-based products would be b oosted thanks to the development of the proposed co ncept and 
thus making possible an improvement of the competit iveness in European Bioeconomy sector as well as to  diversify 
in current models of biorefineries in EU Member states.  
By using the biorefinery concept applied to OFMSW ( urban biorefinery), organic waste will be further e xploited as feedstock 
which currently is not wide and properly valorised along EU. The development of biotechnological proce sses included in the 
project such us anaerobic digestion or bioethanol f ermentation, among others, are a vital part of the European bio-economy 
tapping into organic municipal solid waste to produ ce high value added products. In particular, it wil l be taken into account its 
heterogeneity and variable composition, to produce different valuable marketable products. Then, requi red logistics and supply 
chain for a cascading-in-value/-in-time 
56 use of the waste streams would be developed along t he project and it will contribute to 
the shift towards a resource-efficient, low-carbon economy to achieve sustainable growth (resource-eff icient Europe flagship 
initiative, Europe Strategy 2020).   
Municipalities and companies dealing with organic w aste management, in addition to their fees for trea ting this material, can 
increase their revenue by producing chemical building blocks, biopolymers, additives and/or fertilisers. Helping at the same time 
to fulfil the requirements of Waste Framework Directive which urges to member states by 2020 to take the necessary measures 
to achieve the targets of re-use and recycling of d omestic waste and move towards a European recycling  society with a high 
level of resource efficiency. Thus, reducing the or ganic materials that currently are sent to landfill s and, consequently, not 
converted into valuable products.  
Additionally as it is stated by European Biogas Ass ociation, anaerobic digestion is an important pilla r of the European circular 
economy: it mitigates GHG emissions, recycles nutri ents in the form of organic fertilisers, prevents n itrogen leakage into 
groundwater and avoids the spread of harmful diseases through landfilling.

The project is composed by 8 work packages; each on e follows a logical phase of the project. There is one work package 
(WP1) which addresses all preliminary actions for t he urban biorefinery design. Four mainly technical,  demonstration and 
validation work packages (WP2-WP5) and three work p ackages which support project activities as integra tion of results, LCA, 
LCC regulatory assessments (WP6); communication, di ssemination and exploitation activities (WP7) and p roject management 
(WP8). The inter-relation between the different work packages and Gantt chart are below.
