2015 www.clamber.castillalamancha. 
es 
Technical equipment : 
IRIAF’s infrastructure relevant to the proposed work: 
- UP-STREAM: this area aims to transform raw lignoc ellulosic biomass into a fermentable carbon source or store and adapt 
raw materials (waste or by-products) that can be part of fermentation media. It consists of solid biomass storage (4 x 35 m 3), 
mills for herbaceous and woody biomass (200 kg/h), extractor with vapour (3 m 3), Two-step Steam Explosion Reactor (400 l, 
up to 21 barg) and solid-liquid separators (15 m 3/h). 
- MID-STREAM: this area aims to transform biologica lly (anaerobic digestion, fermentation, etc.) the c arbon source 
generated in Up-stream in different products of interest (biofuels, building blocks, etc.). It consists of Microbiology laboratory 
for micro management (starters, inoculants, etc.), Bioreactors for hydrolysis and anaerobic and aerobic fermentation: 2 x 3 l, 
2 x 30 l, 1 x 300 l, 1 x 3,000 l and 1 x 20,000 l, Systems for sterilization, substrate preparation, a ddition of sterile reactants, 
cleaning in place, and other utilities. 
- DOWN-STREAM: this area is formed by a series of m odules of typical technologies of industrial biotec hnology segment 
ability to interface with each other in order to concentrate and purify the products of interest from the remaining fermentation 
broth. It consists of Harvesting Tanks with capacit y to act as a extractor: 1 x 10,000 l (ATEX), 1 x 1 0,000 l (No ATEX), 1 x 
1,500 l (ATEX) and 1 x 1,500 l (No ATEX), Microfiltration system (1 m3/h) and Centrifugation system (1.5 m 
3/h) (ATEX). 
- ANAEROBIC DIGESTION: It is a modular and transpor table pilot plant for experiments of anaerobic dige stion and co-
digestion of solid and liquid organic biomass to pr oduce biogas and bio-fertilizers. It consists of Li quid storage tank (20 m 3), 
Solid storage hopper (10 m 3), Pasteurization tank (1 m 3), Homogenization Tank (3 m 3), Digester (11 m 3), Digestate tank (5 
m3) and Gasometer (10 m 3). 
- UTILITIES: this area includes all the necessary s ervices for the operation of the bio-refinery units : power supply, steam 
boilers, process water, cooling water (Tower, chill er, glycol), compressed air and instrumentation and  process gases (NH 3, 
N2, O 2, CO 2). 
- WASTE MANAGEMENT: this area includes the necessar y for the treatment and/or storage of liquid and so lid wastes 
produced in the biorefinery facilities previous to the discharge or delivery manager, always prioritiz ing the 3Rs: Reduce, 
Reuse and Recycle. The wastewater treatment is base d on a hybrid constructed wetland system with reeds , where the 
organic compounds of the wastewater that could not be used previously are assimilated and generate a n ew lignocellulosic 
biomass that can be used as a new resource in the plant, closing the cycle. 
- PROCESS CONTROL: this area is a control room where, through a SCADA system, the following activities are carried out: 
processing and monitoring of the process informatio n, remote control devices and settings values in da tabases system, 
alarm monitoring and deployment of processes, repor ting and statistical process data and storage of hi storical data and 
trends of process variables. 
- ANALYTICAL CONTROL PROCESS: It is a laboratory th at supports the activity of the processing and envi ronmental 
units, aimed at providing the necessary techniques to develop most of the assessments and monitoring the operation of the 
different experiments.

Role in the Project:

a) to promote the URBioFIN project and disseminate its results as widely and as effectively as possibl e to all relevant 
stakeholders, the broad public and the scientific community by all modern communication means. 
b) to ensure proper and effective handling of Intellectual Property Rights to manage effectively  expl oitation of project 
results within the consortium as well as with third parties. 
These activities are part of BCM’s daily business and will by performed by their respective experts.

PARTNER 16 
Name : NATRUE AISBL  
Web page 
: http://www.natrue.org/ 
Type: Association 
of  SMEs Country: 
Belgium 
  
Brief description of the organization:  
NATRUE AISBL is a Brussels-based international non- profit association consisting of natural and organi c cosmetics 
manufacturers, and a holder and setter of a private standard in the sector. NATRUE is not a certification body.   
Founded in 2007 by pioneers of the sector including  WELEDA AG, WALA Heilmittel GmbH (Dr.Hauschka Skin Care), 
Laverana GmbH & Co. KG, Primavera Life GmbH, NATRUE  has currently 49 members spread across 25 countrie s. 
Adoption of the NATRUE label on certified products currently exceeds 4.700 products across a total of 204 brands. 
NATRUE’s activities can be grouped into three main areas: 
Advocacy:  NATRUE works to protect and promote natural and or ganic cosmetics and their ingredients, assessing th e 
impact that emerging or evolving regulations will have on the sector and contributing to the discussions with the European 
institutions, always bearing in mind consumer’s expectations and sectorial needs 
Label:  NATRUE helps consumers to identify which products are truly natural and organic in a sector which sti ll lacks a 
legal and harmonized definition. 
Research: 
 NATRUE pools resources to investigate scientific i ssues of concern. Being an association, we gather t he 
expertise of different companies, institutions and other associations to help the sector position itse lf with regards to 
specific topics. 
NATRUE is listed on the EU transparency register un der the identification number: 75060586073-86. NATR UE is a 
liaison organisation representative providing exper t technical opinion to ISO TC/217/WG4 and CEN TC/39 2 for 
cosmetics, and is a member of the EU Commission’s Working Group on Cosmetic Products (E01302). 
 
Role in the Project:   
The Role of NATRUE AISBL is related to the coordination with supporting member companies from the association which 
includes:  
- Development of packaging concepts and specifications for cosmetic applications in WP5. 
- Studies on product-packaging interactions in specific cosmetic product applications in WP5.  
In addition to that, the role of NATRUE AISBL includes the participation in WPs 1, 6, 7 and 8.

4.2  Third parties involved in the project 
 
Recently, IRIAF has mandated the personnel management of the CLAMBER Plant to the public company GEACAM. Therefore 
GEACAM will act as a third party of IRIAF. 
First of all, it should be noted that the IRIAF is the proprietary of the CLAMBER Plant and is in charge of all the costs of the 
project (reactants, consumables, transport of digestate, electricity, tap water, gas, etc.) except the personnel one, which is 
shared with GEACAM. 
IRIAF personnel is in charge of all the administrative works of the project, justification of the project, dissemination, purchasing 
management, which, as a public organization, is governed by the publish procurement law, etc. 
GEACAM personnel will carry out all the technical works of the project, execution of experiments, operation of the different units 
of the plant, analysis of control samples, industrial maintenance of the equipment, etc.

6.  ADDENDUM 1. BUSINESS CASES  
 
BIOETHANOL as Building block 
 
 
1.  Value proposition. 
Ethanol is a colourless, volatile, flammable liquid  produced by yeast fermentation of carbohydrates, o r synthetically, by 
hydration of ethylene. It is used chiefly as a tran sport fuel or fuel additive (replacing fossil gasol ine), with other applications 
being used as heating fuel, a solvent, a chemical industry feedstock, and in beverages, antiseptics and medicines. Bioethanol is 
the largest biofuel with an estimated worldwide pro duction of 100 billion litres in 2013, and with an expected production of 150 
billion litres in 2020 63 .  The growing market for bioethanol as a biofuel has also been accompanied by an increasingly interest as 
a useful platform molecule for the production of value-added chemicals (Figure 6.1). 
                                                           
63  (OECD-FAO, 2014) http://ethanolproducer.com/articles/11264/oecd-fao-outlook-report-predicts-growth-in-ethanol-production 
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 93 
 
 
Bioethanol is mainly produced by yeast fermentation of sugar-rich and starch-rich biomass like sugarcane (Brazil), maize (North 
America) or cereals (Europe). The industrial produc tion of ethanol from second-generation lignocellulo se biomass is rapidly 
developing, with many projects being developed in the US and EU. 
 
 
Figure 6.1 . Ethanol as chermical building block (adapted from ref. 64 ) 
 
2.  Existing alternatives 
93% of total ethanol produced worldwide comes from bio based methods (yeast fermentation), while only 7% is fossil ethanol 
(catalytic hydration of ethylene) 65 . 
 
3.  Price per unit 
Market price evolution of ethanol during last year is shown in Figure 6.2.

4.  Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plan. 
Bioethanol from MSW can be feasible in installations treating 40,000 – 50,000 tonnes of MSW per year, depending mainly of 
the OFMSW quality, and the bioethanol yields obtained in the process (around 100-130 liters of bioethanol from tonne of dry 
MSW tonne). 
 
5.  Market segment 
Main current use of bioethanol is as transport biofuel or fuel additive for gasoline. Bioethanol  has also been used in the 
beverage industry and as renewable chemical building block for the production of ethylene, propylene, diethyl ether, 1,3-
butadiene, acetaldehyde, ethyl acetate, etc. 
67  (Figure 6.3).

Global bioethanol market volume was 100 billion litres in 2014 69 . 
 
7.  Growth rate 
It is expected a bioethanol production of 150 billi on litres in 2020 70 , which means an increase of 50% compared to curren t 
market volumes.  
 
8.  Market trends 
Currently bioethanol is produced from edible biomass, mainly from sugar in Brazil and starch in USA (Figure 6.4).

Figure 6.4 . Production of bioethanol per country or region in 2013 71 . 
 
However, due to the competition with food and conce rns about ecological systems, recent research has s hifted towards 
bioethanol production from non-food biomass feedsto cks (lignocellullosic materials from agricultural, industrial and urban 
wastes), which are low cost and abundantly availabl e sources. Second generation lignocellulosic ethano l is near 
commercialisation, with first-of-a-kind commercial plants built or currently under construction, and i s therefore within the scope 
of the study, although a great deal is known about this industry already, and therefore less effort is afforded it compared to more 
novel pathways to different fuels and chemicals.  
 
9.  Barriers and risks 
The main barriers facing bioethanol production are:  the high cost and energy use in product separation , the stability of yeast 
strains and the low productivity due to substrate/p roduct inhibition and slow degradation of substrate s. These barriers have 
been mitigated by the latest advances in the use of  more efficient downstream processing techniques, n ew strains with 
improved stability and higher productivities and the efficient integration of pre-treatment with fermentation steps 
72 . 
 
10.  Business approach 
IMECAL and CIEMAT have developed a biotechnological  patented technology (European Patent EPO 2112226, USA Patent 
8399228 B2 and international PCT WIPO 099038) for o btaining second generation bioethanol from OFMSW (P ERSEO 
Bioethanol®). The commercialization rights belong t o IMECAL. The bioethanol obtained, currently used a s a biofuel, will be 
used as chemical building block for the conversion to bioethylene and subsequent polymerisation to bioPE. 
 
 
BIO ETHYLENE 
 
 
1.  Value proposition. 
Ethylene is primarily a monomer used as a feedstock in the manufacture of polymer plastics, fibers and other organic chemicals 
that are ultimately consumed in the packaging, tran sportation and construction industries and in a mul titude of industrial and 
consumer markets. Nondurable or consumable end uses —in particular, packaging—make up more than half of  ethylene 
derivative consumption worldwide. One plastic resin  in particular, polyethylene (PE), accounts for the  majority of ethylene 
consumption. Ethylene is one of the largest-volume chemicals worldwide 73 , with a diverse derivative portfolio, including 
nondurable and durable end uses (Figure 6.5). 
 
                                                          
 
71  http://ethanolrfa.org/pages/World-Fuel-Ethanol-Production  
72  http://www.e4tech.com/project/from-the-sugar-platform-to-biofuels-and-biochemical/  
73  https://www.ihs.com/products/ethylene-chemical-economics-handbook.html 
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 96 
 
 
 
Figure 6.5 . Derivative chemicals and polymers from ethylene (adapted from ref. 74 ) 
 
2.  Existing alternatives 
Fossil ethylene is derived from either modifying na tural gas (a methane, ethane, propane mix) or from the catalytic cracking of 
crude oil 75 . Ethylene from renewable raw materials (bio-ethylene) is made by catalytic dehydratation of bio-ethanol 76 . Producing 
ethylene from bioethanol is the only commercial solution for biobased and sustainable downstream production. 
 
3.  Price per unit 
In India and Brazil, where bioethanol feedstocks (t ypically sugarcane) are relatively inexpensive and easily accessible, bio-
ethylene production costs are closer to fossil ones . In Brazil and India they are typically 1,200 $/to nne bio-ethylene, and in 
China (using sweet sorghum feedstocks) are around 1 ,700 $/tonne. In the United States, bioethylene cos ts (from corn) are 
reported at about 2,000 $/tonne, and in Europe (fro m sugar beet) around 2,600 $/tonne. Lignocellulose- based bio-ethylene 
production is estimated at 1,900-2,000 $/tonne in the US 77 . 
 
4.  Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plant. 
Bioethylene can be feasible in any capacity. Howeve r, it depends on the comparison to petroleum based ethylene price, and 
economy of scale has to be taken into account. In g eneral, capacities over 40 KTA are mostly successfu l and feasible plants, 
depending on the downstream products 78 . 
 
5.  Market segment 
Ethylene is mainly used as a monomer for the produc tion of plastics: PE, PET, PEG, PVC and PS. PE, the  largest ethylene 
derivative market, accounted for over 60% of global  ethylene consumption in 2014 79 . Markets include film, packaging, 
containers and articles for home and light industrial use. The next-largest derivative market is ethylene oxide for ethylene glycol 
used primarily to produce PET (used in polyethylene  terephthalate fibers, bottles and other goods). In  2014, ethylene oxide 
accounts for 15% of total world consumption. Ethyle ne dichloride (primarily for vinyl chloride and pol yvinyl chloride resin)

accounted for 10% of the world ethylene market in 2 014. Apart from its use as monomer for plastics, et hylene has also been 
used as fruit ripening agent in agriculture 80 . 
  
6.  Market size 
Total worldwide production of ethylene was 127 mill ion tons in 2014, being one of the most consumed ch emicals 81 . Figure 6.6 
shows world consumption of ethylene per region. 
 
  
Figure 6.6 . World consumption of ethylene per region (2014) 82  
 
Braskem  is the first commercial producer of bioethylene from bioethanol, utilising Brazil’s traditional strengths in producing 
large volumes of low cost sugarcane ethanol for transport. The ethylene produced is processed directly to bioPE 
83 . Annual 
bioethylene production capacity is up to 200 ktpa 84 . 
  
7.  Growth rate 
During 2009–2014, world ethylene consumption grew at an average rate of almost 4.5% per year, while capacity increased at a 
slower rate of about 3.5%. Ethylene consumption is expected to grow at about 4% per year over the next five years. Capacity 
additions will match this growth 85 . 
The global production capacity of bio-based ethylene to produce bioPE is estimated to be able to reach  840 ktonnes by 2020 86 , 
quadrupling current capacities.  
The improved GHG and fossil energy use performance of bio-based PE, together with increasing focus on sustainability and 
more stringent environmental regulations and targets, is anticipated to drive demand for bio-based ethylene. 
 
8.  Market trends 
Bioethylene has so far been successfully produced i n Brazil, where ethanol is relatively inexpensive a nd easily available. 
Cheaper production of second generation bioethanol from alternative sources (lignocellulosic ethanol f rom industrial, 
agricultural and urban wastes) is expected to expand the bioethylene production market to different regions.  
When it comes to bio-plastics (the biggest end use of bio-ethylene), according to BCCC research the market size will grow from 
$387.6 billion in 2013, to $700.7 billion in 2018. Demand for biobased products is growing due to: 
- Corporate social responsibility 
- Sustainability by manufacturers 
- Petroleum dependency 
- Environmental responsibility 
- Demand for renewable 
- Brand value

9.  Barriers and risks 
Barriers and risks to the bio-based ethylene market  expanding are associated with the bioPE market, si nce it is the main 
derivative product (see bioPE business case). 
 
10.  Business approach 
Bioethylene will be produced from catalytic dehydra tion of second generation bioethanol from OFMSW. In  this case the 
business model is based on the technology licensing for the catalytic conversion from bioethanol to bioethylene. Bioethylene will 
be used to produce bioPE and also as a fruit ripeni ng agent.  IP rights on the associated technologies  will belong to IMECAL 
(bioethanol from MSW) and GI dynamics (Bioehylene technology). 
 
 
MEDIUM-CHAIN FATTY ACIDS (C8-C14) AS BUILDING BLOCK
 
 
1.  Value proposition. 
Medium-chain fatty acids with carbon chain lengths varying from 8 to 14 have a broad range of applicat ions as lubricants, 
creams, soaps, antimicrobials, and edible oils. The y are not constituents of fossil fuels and relative  complicated chemical 
processes are needed to make them from longer-chain  fatty acids. Coconut and palm kernel oil are the n atural sources for 
these fatty acids with coconut oil consisting for a lmost 50% of lauric acid (C12), 16% myristic acid ( C14), 8% decanoic acid 
(C10) and 7% caprylic acid (C8). Both coconut and p alm are under extreme pressure as source for these MCFA’s because of 
their additional use as food materials (competition  for food) and their high impact on the environment  (land/water use, 
agricultural waste). Alternative sources that can b e produced using more sustainable technologies, suc h as yeast and 
microalgae, are in increasing demand. 
Caprylic acid is a strong antimicrobial/antifungal and is died as ingredient to soaps and cosmetic pre parations exactly for that 
purpose 
Decanoic acid, as sodium salt, is widely applied in soap. In addition is used in perfumes, lubricants and plastics. 
Lauric acid is mainly applied as cleansing agent (soap) because of its surfactant properties and strong antimicrobial activity. 
Myristic acid is applied, usually as ester, in cosmetics as a surfactant, cleansing, opacifying and thickening agent.       
 
2.  Existing alternatives 
Nowadays, coconut oil and palm kernel oil are the m ajor sources for lauric acid. Myristic acid is isol ated from coconut, nutmeg 
and babassu palm. Caprylic acid and ecanoic acid are isolated from coconut oil and goat milk fat. The major end-users of these 
MCFA’s are large Food, Personal Care and Chemical companies such as Unilever, Proctor & Gamble, AKZONobel and Mitsui. 
  
3.  Price per unit 
Caprylic acid is sold for prices ranging from 3 to 12 $ kg 
-1 depending on its purity and application (in cosmetics/soaps or foods). 
Decanoic acid is generally sold in the price range of 4-6 $ kg -1 to the flavour and fragrance industry. 
Lauric acid market price varies from 3 € kg -1 for detergent application to 5-10 € kg -1 for cosmetic application to > 20 € kg -1 for 
food application. 
Myristic acid market price varies from 3-5 € kg 
-1 for applications in skin oil to over 10 € kg -1 for food applications. 
 
4.  Market segment 
Caprylic acid is widely used in the cosmetic, food and feed industry for its antimicrobial activity. 
Decanoic acid is applied in the chemical industry f or its lubrication properties, but also in the cosm etic industry as lubricant but 
also because of its flavour properties. 
Lauric acid, as sodium salt, is soap and as such is  applied in most detergents, soaps and other cleans ing agents used in the 
Home & Personal Care and Cosmetic industry. It is a lso more-and-more applied as antimicrobial ingredie nt in Food & Feed 
Industry. 
Myristic acid is mostly applied in the Cosmetic Ind ustry as a surfactant, cleansing, opacifying and th ickening agent. Has also 
emulsifying properties. 
 
5.  Market size 
The total fatty acid market size is expected to hit  12,000 M€ in 2017, about 40% of this market in the  personal care (detergent, 
soap, cosmetics) sector. The current market size for caprylic acid is approximately 2.5 M€. The market size for decanoic acid is 
approximately 50 M€, for lauric acid approximately 2,000 M€ and for myristic acid approximately 600 M€. 
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 99 
 
 
 
6.    Growth rate 
The fatty acid market size is expected to double in the next 5-6 years in both the cosmetic and food applications. 
 
7.  Market trends 
Growth of coconut and palm trees for its production  of fatty acids/oils is steadily growing in tropica l countries. This has resulted 
in massive reduction of natural tropical forest are as and general negative sentiment about consumer pr oducts based on these 
crops. Alternative sources such as (micro-)algae an d also several production processes based on synthe tic biology/genetic 
engineering are rapidly penetrating the market.  
 
8.  Barriers and risks 
The main barrier for the microbial MCFA market is the price difference with the coconut/palm-derived fatty acids.  
 
9.  Business approach 
MCFA’s will be isolated from microalgae used for CO
2/NH 3-stripping of the biogas and by growth of various Cryptococcus  
variants on the VFA produced by the anaerobic digesters.  
 
 
PHA 
 
 
1.  Value proposition  
The high value of the PHA is based not only in the bioBased production process itself, but also in its  biodegradable nature, as 
seen in following picture (Figure 6.7). Biobased po lymers such as PHA are generally more sustainable m aterials than their 
petrochemical-based counterparts,  and,  as  previo usly  mentioned,  they can  be  produced  from  a  wide  range  of  
renewable resources. 
 
 
Figure 6.7.  Procedure and biodegradability of plastic materials 
 
 
Additionally, the PHAs represent the only class of thermoplastic polymers which are converted directly by microorganisms from 
feedstock without necessity of additional polymeris ation steps. Moreover due to its characteristics th e PHAs can replace some 
of the most common fossil-fuel plastics, such as: P ET (Polyethylene terephthalate), LDPE (Low Density Polyethylene), HDPE 
(High Density Polyethylene), and PP (Polypropylene)  such is consider in the image below from Lund et a l. These four types of 
plastics currently represent more than 50% of worldwide plastics production (as depicted in Figure 6.8).  
 
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 100 
 
 
 
Figure 6.8. Potential replacement for PHA of the current plastics 
 
2.  Existing alternatives  
The sustainable sourcing of feedstock is a prerequi site for more sustainable products. This is the mai n objective of PHA 
production in this project, since normally typical PHA comes from food competing source such as the us e of corn fields for the 
production of starch as raw material for polymers. Negative impacts caused by bad agricultural practic e must be avoided, 
mainly when apply social criteria and human rights.  The environmental impact of plastic production sho uld be also avoided; 
therefore the other alternative to produce PHA coming from fossil fuels is not a good choice.  
Regarding prices the PHA costs from residual stream s can be reduced in order to make the process more competitive with 
these not desired alternatives, mainly respect to the plastic produced by crude oil with a market price around 1.1 kg crude oil kf -1 
polymer. 
 
3.  Price per unit  
Depending on which bacterial producer is used to generate PHA, the cost of production can range from $4-$16 per kg. 
However, the price should be $3-$5 per kg to be commercially viable. 
The objective in this project is: 
- 8-9 € kg 
-1 for mcl-PHA 
- 4-5 € kg -1 for scl-PHA 
Since the future market for mcl-PHA allow higher prices. 
 
4.  Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plan  
The bioplastic produced from module 2, coming from VFA of the first stage of AD will represent around 0.5 ton per year. 
For module 3, bioplastic coming from biomethane, the product obtained will be 0.5 ton per year. 
 
5.  Market segment  
The actual market segments of PHA consist in flexible packagings and mulch films (used in blends), and some niche markets in 
consumer goods (cosmetic packagings, horticulture p ots, biodegradable cutlery, etc.). The most represe ntative PHAs are 
polyhydroxybutyrate (PHB) and poly-hydroxy valerate  (PHV) and its copolymer (PHBV), with physical prop erties similar to 
polyethylene and polypropylene. 
In both markets, besides its physical properties, P HA provides biodegradability. This is particularly adapted to these markets 
where the product shelf-life is low. In particular in the case of mulch films, a major advantage arise s in this case in addition to 
having the advantages of a traditional plastic film: after use there is no need of collecting the film as it will degrade in soil. 
 
 
 
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 101 
 
 
6.  Market size  
Bioplastics  are  generating  increasing  interest  for industries  and  their  market  is  rising  as   a  result  of technological  
advances  and  cost  reductions 
In 2014, the global production capacities of biopla stics amounted to 1.7 million tons. This translates  to approximately 680,000 
hectares of land.  
The key players in the PHA market are Biomatera, Bi omer, Bio-On SROL, Kaneka corporation, Meredian Ins ., Metabolix Inc, 
Newlight Technologies, LIC, PHA industrial S.A., Po lyferm Canada Inc., Shenzhen Ecomann Biotech Co Ltd , Tianan Biologic 
Materials Co and Tianiin Greenbio Materials Co Ltd. The global capacity in 2014 by segments is showed in the Figure below. 
 
 
 
Figure 6.9. Global production capacities of bioplastics 2014 
 
PHA could be used in various applications, but the targeted markets in the project are flexible packag ing, consumer goods and 
agriculture/horticulture. The actual potential mark et size can then be estimated at around 80,000 tons : 35,000 tons in flexible 
packaging (partial substitution of PLA and PLA blen ds, starch blends and other biodegradable), 35,000 tons in 
agriculture/horticulture (partial substitution of s tarch-blends) and 10,000 tons in consumer goods. Du e to their properties, scl-
and mcl- PHA should in particular be a good substit ute to PBAT, a fossil-based biopolymer currently us ed for improving 
softness and processability of starch-based and PLA -based blends. PBAT has today a global production c apacity of around 
80,000 tons per year. 
 
7.  Growth rate  
The growth rate from 2014 to 2019 expected can be s een in next Figure. It is expected a growth in PHA market from 32 to 104 
Mton, mainly related to flexible or rigid packaging and agriculture purposes.

8.  Market trends  
Rising demand of biodegradable materials, positive attitude of governments towards green procurement p olicies, and rising oil 
prices are attracting companies to invest in PHA (P olyhydroxyalkanoate) market. Many large companies s uch as Meredian 
(U.S.), Ecomann (China), and Bio-on (Italy) are foc using on increasing production capacities. Europe is expected to be the one 
of the largest PHA market over the next six years. Gaining importance of biodegradable plastics coupled with regulatory support 
is expected to favor market growth in Europe. Asia Pacific would emerge as a promising market in the n ear future. Low 
production cost as compare to North America and Eur ope coupled with application growth in agriculture,  food & beverage and 
cosmetics in China and India is expected to have a positive impact on PHA market in the near future. 
 
9.  Barriers and risks  
The main barrier is the price difference between fo ssil and bio-based polymers, and competition with o ther bio-based polymers 
such as starch-based blends and PBAT in flexible pa ckaging and agriculture/horticulture, and PLA in co nsumer goods. PHA 
here produced will have however some significant ad vantages against other biobased polymers: a non-foo d competing and 
sustainable resource, and an increased biodegradability. 
 
10.  Business approach 
PHA will be produced by VFAs coming from the partial AD of OFMSW, as well as from the biogas coming from the second step 
of AD process. In this case the business model is based on the technology licensing for both modules 2 and 3 with the IP rights 
belonging to URBASER, IRIAF, WR, AINIA and Nature Plast. 
 
 
 
 
COMPOSITE BIOMATERIALS 
 
 
1.  Value proposition. 
Composite materials as used f.i in the construction , automotive and chemical industries, combine attri butes from two of more 
different materials in one combined application. Ex amples are the combination of (bio-)plastics with n atural materials/fibers 
coming from wood, wheat or starch. This, generally,  leads to materials that combine the attributes of plastics (strength, low 
price, high T-tolerance) and the appearance, feel a nd smell of natural materials. A good example is WP C (Wood Plastic 
Composites) which has a current market value of 4,0 00 M€ per year and combines wood fibre with PE, PP or PVC. Within the 
100% biobased solutions, combination of PLA, PHA, c ellulose fibres and algae biomass are examples of c omposites e.g. used 
in 3D-color print applications. By replacing PE and  other plastics by biobased alternatives as offered  in this project, new 100% 
biobased solutions will also be offered for the bulk composite applications.   
 
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 103 
 
 
2.  Existing alternatives 
The current plastics used in composite materials – PE, PVC, PP, PBS – are all fossil-fuel based. They are generally (much) 
lower in price than the biobased alternatives. 
 
3.  Price per unit 
The price would depend completely on the type of ma terials used for the composite and its application (10 € kg 
-1). E.g. WPC is 
cheaper than 100% wood materials. 
 
4.  Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plant. 
Within the UrBioFin-project, we will be producing s ufficient composite materials based on PE, PHA, cel lulose and lignin to 
develop various biobased packaging solutions for cosmetics and also various plastic bag-prototypes with different flexibilities. 
 
5.  Market segment 
PE is widely used in the automotive industry, cosmetics manufactory, packaging (plastic bags, films, containers), toys, personal 
hygiene and cleaning products. The composite materi als based on PE and natural fibres (cellulose, wood  fibre/lignin, etc) are 
forming a rapidly growing market with a market value for WPC alone of 4,000 M € per year. 
 
6.  Market size 
The total packaging market in Europe is approximate ly 130 billion €. Over 50% of this market is biobas ed (paper, board, wood) 
and 31% is plastic. The major plastic packaging mat erial is PE-based (56%) for mostly films, wraps, an d bags but also some 
bottles and containers (HD-PE) and smaller amounts of PP (19%), PET (11%), PS (10%) and PVC (4%) are used.  
In 2012, in the Netherlands, only 1 ktonne of bioplastic (0.036%) was used.  
For packaging, very few composite materials are cur rently available. However the possibilities are ext remely high since 
composites potentially can combine the favourable attributes of various compounds, e.g. the light weight and strength of plastic 
with the abundant availability and flexibility of paper and board. 
 
7.  Growth rate 
The growth rate of biobased packaging materials is expected to be very high in the next 10 years, with EU-legislation restricting 
the use of fossil-based plastic bags in Supermarkets, etc.    
 
8.  Market trends 
Bio-based PE has so far been successfully produced only in Brazil, due to the presence of large quantities of low cost feedstock 
(with Brazil the leading producer of sugar cane eth anol globally). Cheaper production of second genera tion bioethanol from 
alternative sources (lignocellulosic ethanol from i ndustrial, agricultural and urban wastes) is expect ed to expand the PE 
production market to different regions.  
 
9.  Barriers and risks 
The main barrier to the bio-based PE market expandi ng is the price difference between fossil and bio-b ased. This is partially 
due to externalisation of environmental costs for f ossil products, and internalising these costs (for example via green VAT or a 
carbon tax) could reduce the price difference with fossil PE and make bio-based PE a more competitive product. Further 
incentive, in the form of volatile crude oil prices  together with limited supply, had opened a market gap for bio-based chemicals 
at large including bio-based PE. In addition to cos t competitiveness, there are also uncertainties whi ch may hamper bio-based 
PE deployment. These include feedstock availability concerns (where raw materials such as sugarcane ar e also widely used in 
other industries), and competition with other competing bio-based packaging polymers such as PET, PLA and PEF 
87 . 
 
10.  Business approach 
Bio-PE will be produced by polymerisation of bioeth ylene coming from dehydration of second generation bioethanol from 
OFMSW. The scl-PHA will be produced using existing mixed culture fermentation and fed with the VFA-fra ction from the 
anaerobic fermentor and mcl-PHA will be produced by  fermentation of Pseudomonas putida  using MCFA deriving from the 
elongation fermentor as feed. Composite materials w ill be developed/manufactured by the Biomaterial re search group of WR. 
Bio-PE, mcl-PHA and scl-PHA and various natural mat erials present in the MSW (cellulose, lignin) will be blended/extruded in 
various applications. 
                                                          
 
87  http://www.grandviewresearch.com/industry-analysis/bio-based-polyethylene-pe-market

BIOMETHANE  
 
1.  Value proposition. 
Natural gas (NG) plays nowadays an important role i n energy provision in the EU countries to meet the demand for heat, 
energy and transport fuels. NG is a fossil based fu el which significantly contributes to greenhouse ga s emissions and to the 
depletion of finite resources. In this sense, biomethane is an attractive renewable alternative that can be used as a substitute for 
transport fuels or energy production, or even as fe edstock to obtain chemicals and added value product s. Biomethane can be 
obtained from the upgrading of biogas. In this proc ess, other gases contained in the biogas apart from  CH 4 (CO 2, H 2S, VOCs, 
siloxanes, etc.) are removed in order to meet the required standards for its injection into natural gas grid.  
Despite the advantages and future prospects of biom ethane, key hotspots to guarantee the sustainabilit y of the process have 
been identified in the biomethane value chain. Amon g them, the feedstock for biogas production and pro vision and the 
conversion process of biogas to biomethane (which still represents ~25-30% of the total biomethane costs) will be addressed in 
URBIOFIN project.   
 
2.  Existing alternatives 
EU energy consumption is nowadays mainly covered wi th fossil based fuels. For instance, from the 1683 Mtoe consumed in 
2012 in inland countries, crude oil and petrol prod ucts represented 33.8% and the share of natural gas  was 23.4%. The other 
two main contributors to the total consumption were solid fuels (17.5%) and nuclear energy (13.5%) 
88 . 
 
3.  Price per unit 
Natural gas is currently sold at 0.3-0.4 € m -3, approximately 50% lower than the actual price of biomethane (~0.6 € m -3). The 
main contributors to biomethane price (including op erational and capital costs of the supply chain) ar e the cost of raw biogas 
production and the upgrading costs, with a lower share of the distribution and refuelling stations 1. 
 
4.  Amount that will be feasible per year with the upgraded pilot plant. 
In the prototype photobioreactor, an average algal biomass productivity of 11-13 g m -2d-1 is expected. Based on previous 
experimental results from the UVA research group, between 7.8 and 9.2 Nm 3 of biogas will be daily upgraded to biomethane.  
 
5.  Market segment 
The potential uses of biomethane are similar to tho se of natural gas: production of power and heat, di rect use as automotive 
fuel, and feedstock for the production of many different products (paints, plastics, detergents, etc.). 
 
6.  Market size 
About 3.8 × 10 11 m3 of natural gas were used in 2014 for primary energy  consumption. In contrast, current production of 
biomethane accounts for 1.4 Bn m 3, of which only 10% is used in transport. Therefore , there is a potential market available for 
biomethane as a substitute of natural gas. 
 
7.  Growth rate 
According to the European Biogas Association, in 20 14 there were 367 biomethane plants in Europe, of w hich 70% injected to 
natural gas grids. These plants exhibit a biomethan e upgrading capacity of 200,000 Nm 
3 h-1, producing yearly ~ 1.4 Bn m 3 of 
biomethane.This biomethane upgraded from biogas represents a very low percentage of the total biogas production (only 2% of 
the total biogas was upgraded in 2010), however rec ent estimations from the European Biogas Association revealed that about 
37.5 % of the total production of biogas will be up graded to biomethane in 2030 (18 Bn m 3) and used for grid injection and fuel. 
The advantages of this product in terms of energy s ustainability and reduction of GHG emissions will c ertainly contribute to the 
growth of the European biomethane market.  
 
8.  Market trends 
So far, biogas upgrading is mainly undertaken in Eu rope, with minor upgrading activities in the USA or  South Korea 
89 . The 
biomethane production market is expected to expand promoted by a cheaper conversion of the biogas obtained from OMSW to 
biomethane. This expansion will be especially signi ficant in those countries with a good natural gas i nfrastructure, where the 
integration of biomethane into the existing energy system will be relatively easy. 
                                                           
88  http://ec.europa.eu/energy/sites/ener/files/documents/2014_energy_market_en.pdf 
89  http://www.bioenergytrade.org/downloads/t40-t37-biomethane-2014.pdf 
Associated with document Ref. Ares(2017)2272851 - 03/05/2017
                                                                             BBI 2016-D6                                               
  
745785 URBIOFIN - Part B - 105 
 
 
 
9.  Barriers and risks 
Potential limiting factors and risks associated to biomethane include different sections of the biomet hane value chain. One of 
the main concerns is the use of feedstock for energ etic purposes, and many biogas plants are located o n farms with little 
economic advantages for biogas upgrading. URBIOFIN propose the use of OMSW for biogas production and s ubsequent 
upgrading, thus ensuring the sustainability of the feedstock. Moreover, the upgrading system, based on  a biological 
photosynthetic process, will provide additional treatment of the digestate thus reducing the environmental impacts of this waste. 
The low economic efficiency of the biomethane mainl y associated to biogas production and upgrading cos ts also hinder the 
actual competitiveness of biomethane as a substitut e of natural gas. The approach here proposed will s ignificantly reduce the 
costs entailed by the cleaning process by substitut ing current physical-chemical upgrading technologie s with cost-efficient 
biological methods.  
 
10.  Business approach 
Biogas will be first produced by anaerobic digestio n of OMSW, and further upgraded to biomethane by a photosynthetic based 
technology to reach the required quality for its use as automotive fuel. The model business is based on the technology licensing 
for the upgrading of biogas to a biomethane that ca n be used as a non-fossil fuel substituting natural  gas. Intellectual property 
rights on the associated technologies belong to Uni versidad de Valladolid and Urbaser, the terms defin ed in the Consortium 
agreement. 
 
 
SOLID AND LIQUID BIOFERTILISERS 
 
 
1.  Value proposition. 
The URBIOFIN is based on the recovery of waste mate rials, (OFMSW in this case with an estimated annual  generation in the 
EU of  100,000,000 t per year), using the downstrea m intermediate and final products from bioEthanol a nnd bioMethane 
producion. 
Stabilised organic matter solid fraction from End-o f-Waste separate collection, can be an excellent ra w material for elaboration 
of substrates, organic fertilisers and biofertilisers, with the requirements of no impurities and pollutants present, low conductivity, 
no re-fermentation and temperature rise episodes.  
In this case, thanks to the expertise of different partners (NOVO, AINIA, BPE, WR, others) it is propo sed the development of a 
line of solid biofertilisers that will be produced,  characterised, tested in laboratory, greenhouse an d field and throughly 
evaluated. These segment of solid biofertilisers is  more competitive with traditional mineral and orga no-mineral fertilisers, with 
greater size  but lower value added. 
Inoculants, as selected bacteria strains will be su pplied inside URBIOFIN partners or thans to the coo peration and commercial 
links established by the Task leader, BPE, with different companies and research centres.    
Regarding liquid biofertilisers, microalgae grown o n AD liquid and gaseous streams will be used as pro tein source for 
aminoacids production via hydrolysis, to be used as  main component of liquid biofertilisers. Additives  and or inoculants could 
also be added for specific formulations and designe d products. Liquid biofertilisers in general and ba sed in aminoacid and 
peptids in particular, are a fast growing but small er market but with much higher value added products , designated typically for 
fertigation and fruit and vegetables intensive crops.

2.  Existing alternatives 
In the biofetilisers market there is already a grow ing group of companies offering their products to t he market. The market is

innovative companies offering their products, normally diverse from case to case and difficult to compare. 
In the specific case of liquid fertilisers composed of aminoacids and/or polypeptides instead the market has been developing for 
the last 40 years, and there are many medium size a nd big companies offering this type of product, som e of them departing 
from macroalgae and sea weeds, many of them based i n Asia. Some of the international market leading co mpanies are the

Production of solid and liquid biofertilisers will be a sideline of the “OFMSW to Bioethanol” demo pla nt. Production of these 
products will be performed at “Algodor R&D&I and Experimental Production Centre” in Toledo Spain. The capacity of production 
in this Centre is about 2,500 tonnes per year of so lid biofertilisers. Capacity for production of Liqu id biofertilisers is about 1,000 
tonnes per year, but could easily be increased in c ase of necessity, although the limiting factor for the capacity production in 
URBIOFIN project will be the production of microalg ae in the photobioreactor for biomethane upgrading set up at URBASER 
research and development centre in Zaragoza, so a b igger installation for microalgae production at low  cost should be 
implemented in this case.

5.  Market segment 
Biofertilizers also known as “plant probiotics”, by  its favourable effects through the interaction bet ween microorganisms, plants  
and the environment  represent a segment of agricultural inputs (seeds, agri-chemicals, pesticides, etc), with a great potential of 
substitution of the conventional agrichemicals. The y have particular characteristics regarding the abs ence of involvement of 
chemical and/or synthetic products in its fabricati on and consequently those products are suitable for  its use in the organic or 
biological farming. 
Due to their novelty and rapid growing, Biofertilisers and Biostimulants are lacking of a regulatory frame in most Member States 
and in the EU, although it is foreseen its regulation in the revision of Directive 2003/2003 CE on Fertilisers.  
There are different associations at Member State, E uropean or international level that integrate the c ompanies participating in 
this emergent segment of the production of agricultural inputs: 
- AEFA - Asociación Española de Fabricantes de Agro-nutrientes 
http://aefa-agronutrientes.org/  
- EBIC - European Biostimulants Industry Consortium http://www.biostimulants.eu/  
- IBMA – International Biocontrol Manufacturers Association www.ibma-global.org/  
Regarding its application field or method categories of biofertilisers could be stated as follows: 
Types by effect: 
• Nitrogen-fixing 
• Phosphate-solubilising 
• Potash-mobilizing 
• Others (zinc, boron, and sulfur-solubilising biofertilisers) 
By crops: 
• Cereals & grains 
• Pulses & oilseeds 
• Fruits & vegetables 
• Others (turf & ornamentals, plantation crops, fiber crops, spices, and silage & forage crops) 
By microorganisms components: 
• Rhizobium 
• Azotobacter 
• Azospirillum 
• Cyanobacteria 
• Phosphate solubilising bacteria 
• Others (potash-mobilizing, zinc biofertilisers, sulfur-solubilising biofertilisers, and manganese solubiliser) 
By application types: 
• Seed 
• Soil 
• Others (set treatment, foliar treatment, root dipping, and seedling root treatment) 
 
6.  Market size, growth rate and market trend 
The increasing awareness regarding health and welln ess, the growing organic food industry, and the sig nificant increase in the 
price of chemicals have played a huge role in provi ding the necessary boost to the global biofertilise rs industry. Global 
Biofertilisers Market is expected to reach USD 1.88  Billion by 2020 at a CAGR of 14.0% from 2015 to 20 20. The phosphate-
solubilising segment is the fastest- growing market in the biofertilisers industry across the globe.

Figure 6.12. Biofertilisers global market size by types  
 
The market was dominated by North America in 2014 and accounted for the largest share in the total biofertilisers market. Asia-
Pacific is projected to be the fastest-growing mark et for between 2015 and 2020 due to the increasing prices of chemical 
fertilizers and increasing food demand due to popul ation growth. In terms of type, the nitrogen fixing  biofertilisers segment is 
projected to be the largest market for biofertilisers; whereas in terms of crop-type, cereals & grains is projected to be the largest 
market for biofertilisers in 2014. The cyanobacteri a segment is projected to be the largest market for  biofertilisers by 2020 in 
terms of microorganisms. Furthermore, by applicatio n, seed treatment is projected to be the largest ma rket for biofertilisers in 
between 2015 and 2020. 
The biofertilisers market is characterized by intense competition due to the presence of a large number of both large and small-
scale firms. Expansions, acquisitions, agreements and new product launches are the key strategies adop ted by market players 
to ensure their growth in the market. 
 
7.  Barriers and risks 
The main barriers and risks for the development of this value proposition are the following: 
- The risk for competition and price/efficiency barrier with other products already present in the market, with whom newly 
designed and produced biofertilisers have to be compared in tests and field trials.   
- The foreseen revision of Directive 2003/2003 CE on Fertilisers, that will regulate Biofertilisers and Biostimulants, not 
regulated yet. 
- The future costs of product development and registration at EU and international level. 
- The challenge of producing relatively high amounts of microalgae at a low cost, for liquid aminoacids biofertiliser 
production. 
- The risk of effectively and safely perform the fiel d trials in order to obtain a reliable evaluation o f the newly designed 
and produced biofertilisers, and the need of repetitive consistent good results at these evaluation tests. 
 
8.  Business approach 
The solid and liquid biofertilisers valorisation line at URBIOFIN is a sideline of the main “demo” Pla nt for OFMSW conversion in 
bioethanol and the use of bioethanol as building block for other added value chemicals (bioplastics, bioethylene, etc). 
Using as raw materials the potential enormous amoun ts of stabilised liquid and solid biowaste coming f rom the fermentation / 
AD processes, is open a new window for the valorisa tion of these residuals and sidestreams of biowaste  management via the 
Biorefinery concept, in which the connection betwee n waste management companies (URBASER, BPE), techno logy suppliers 
(IMECAL, CIEMAT), biotechnology companies (NOVOZYME S), RTOs (CIEMAT, AINIA, WR) and industries and SME s 
companies, should be adjusted and still work beyond the project duration. 
The selected and separated origin of the biowaste a nd the complex and careful selection and stabilisat ion process should 
guarantee the absence of impurities and pollutants, that would open the possibility of registration of the final products by End-of-
Waste label (out of the waste management scope regulations). 
Stable alliances between involved partners with the  inclusion of specific inoculants or production tec hnology suppliers and 
RTOs could lead to develop a long term successful business strategy for the production and commercialisation of biofertilisers.
